## all-grass farming
 
 <i>n.</i> (Rural) the using of grass, silage and hay for winter feed [rather than supplementary cropping or proprietary feeding.]

>  .. it was found that crops could be dispensed with almost entirely. That gave rise to the era of all-grass farming. <i>(Aug 16, 1948,NZ Journal of Agriculture,133</i>)

>  .. a policy of all-grass farming on light volcanic land has been in operation for the past 15 years. <i>(Jan 15, 1953,NZ Journal of Agriculture,2</i>)

>  For many years root crops and lucerne were grown for supplementary feed, but a start was made towards all-grass farming in 1936. <i>(1953,NZ Journal of Agriculture,3</i>)

>  With the impetus of more intensified all-grass farming, accentuated by labour difficulties, the Dominion acreage of mangolds has progressively declined. <i>(1953,Crop Production Bulletin,277/61</i>)

>  All-grass farming, to be fully effective, requires a well-developed and farmed property. <i>(Jul 8, 1971,NZ Farmer,53</i>)

>  I wanted to go all-grass farming and the pasture was terrible. <i>(1987,NZ Journal of Agriculture,19</i>)



## all-grass system
 
<b>1</b> <i>n.</i> (Rural) a use of grass, silage and hay for winter feed [ rather than supplementary cropping or proprietary feeding.]

>  The Dodunskis had been on an all-grass system at their last job ... <i>(May 10, 2001,Taranaki Daily News,13</i>)

>  .. 60 bail rotary dairy, farmed under an all grass system. [all grass system] <i>(Mar 16, 2002,Dominion,47</i>)

>  Land drainage, wintering pads and more subdivision are all parts of a successful all-grass system. <i>(Jul 8, 1971,NZ Farmer,53</i>)

>  Mr Parks runs an all-grass system with winter ewe management a mixture of strip and block grazing. <i>(Jun 15, 1994,NZ Farmer,31</i>)

>  Pauline favours an all-grass system and employs a 24-hour grazing policy for all but a couple of smaller paddocks on the farm. <i>(Apr 19, 2001,Taranaki Daily News,13</i>)

## alley
 
 <i>n.</i> a marble [1881 but prob. since 1940s obs: (but cf American alley), OED 1720; DARE US 1848, now confined chiefly to the NE states; EDD the ring in which some marble games are played.] [AND alley 2. phrs. 1924.] make one's alley good (with) var. of to make one's marble good, to ingratiate onself (with); to improve one's prospects.]

>  <b>HISLOP</b> Ginger's heart was good, ... but he just did not have that little Hollywood way of whispering sweet nothings down Rosie's neck, and he wanted me - to use his own words - to make his ally [?sic] good and put in a good word for him. <i>(1943,Pure Gold,18</i>)

>  <b>SARGESON</b> `Oh, come on in, mate,' he said. `I don't get the strength of you, but you might be able to make your alley good with mister.' <i>(1965,Memoirs of a Peon,40</i>)

>  <b>KEINZLY</b> Gus had prayed every prayer he could think of that Knocker would do it so that he could give back to Knocker the seven marbles he had won from him by cheating. ... That afternoon he did a boy's sums for seven allys and a tor. <i>(1960,Tangahano,10</i>)

>  <b>SLATTER</b> ... Bob Scott races up and boots the ball into touch. `Trying to make his alley good,' sniffs Spandau. `Shouldn't of missed that penalty.' <i>(1970,On the Ball,138</i>)

>  The board, ever since the tragedy occurred, has spared no pains to whitewash itself and keep its reputation as a "competent" body of administrators unsullied. "N.Z. Truth" has shown in previous articles the lengths to which the board went in its own farcical inquiry to make its alley good with the ratepayers. <i>(Feb 1, 1927,NZ Truth,5</i>)

>  Mackay then made an endeavor to make his alley good, but didn't quite succeed. He said one of the other men in the shop parted up the money for the "rigger." <i>(Jan 22, 1910,NZ Truth,7</i>)

## Alliance
 
 <i>n.</i> [See also "Socialist Alliance Party"]

>  The Alliance was born in St Saviour's Hall, Sydenham on May 1st 1989. It was called NewLabour, but names are unimportant in this story.<i> Ibid. </i> 21 The Alliance represents a new departure in New Zealand political organisation, It is perhaps the first "confederal" party in New Zealand political history... The constitutional structure of the Alliance itself is, to say the least, rudimentary., In broad brush terms, the 5 party coalition (NewLabour, Mana Motuhake, the Democrats, the Liberals and the Greens) is organized as follows... <i> Ibid.</i> 24 By December 1991 both the Democrat's (sic) and the NewLabour Party's annual conference had endorsed the concept of "The Alliance" - an electoral pact in which the parliamentary seats would be divided up between the coalition parties - thus concentrating the minor parties' electoral strength behind a single candidate. <i>(Nov 1992,NZ Political Review,1(8) : 20</i>)

>  The Alliance parties came together to sign their charter on December 1, 1990, largely because of the economic and social crisis New Zealand is entering... "With an Alliance Government, the social contract between people and the government will be upheld, not downgraded." <i>(Dec 1992,Te Maori News,1(11) : 6</i>)

>  Alliance leader-elect Jim Anderton is ecstatic at the coalition's showing in the latest Nationa Business Review poll... Alliance support is strongest in Auckland. <i>(Nov 13, 1992,National Business Review,4</i>)

>  The Alliance plans to name a quarter of its candidates for next year's general election by Christmas. Alliance spokesman Mike Smith said candidates for about 25 seats should be selected this year. <i>(Nov 14, 1992,Evening Post,9</i>)

>  There's nothing that the Alliance thrills to more than the challenge of getting up in Parliament to oppose a really complicated piece of deregulatory or privatisation law that it has only just been allowed to see three minutes ago. <i>(Apr 18, 1998,Listener (New Zealand Listener),17</i>)

>  They know I belong to the Alliance and they get the willies when they think about the Alliance. <i>(Dec 1996,North & South,95</i>)

## allotment
 
 <i>n.</i> 1. A piece of Crown land granted to a particular person for a specified purpose. 2. A piece of land: in towns, a building block; in the country an area use for pasture or cultivation.] [1. Hist. [AND Hist. [Spec.use of allotment allotted portion of land: see OED 4.]]

>  The memorial [from Mr.Sinclair, Wellington] states that certain town lots ... had been advertised for sale by auction, but that several allotments, comprising some of the most valuable lands in the township, ... had been reserved from such sale ...; that these sections were to be paid for at the average price of half the town sections. <i>(Sep 10, 1841,GBPP,130</i>) [NOTES: Villiers and Lefevre Colonial Land and Emigration Office in GBPP 1842 (No.569) 10 Sep 130 [? an allotting : cf LOT]]

>  By the Government advertisement for the sale of town lands at Auckland, it was stated that sections ... would be exposed to sale by auction, but we have since learned that several allotments, comprising some of the most valuable lands in the township, have been reserved fromsuch sale ...; and that these sections are to be paid for, not according to the price ... of the less valuable lots in their vicinity ... but at the average price of half the town sections ... <i>(Feb 10, 1841,GBPP,131</i>) [NOTES: D. Sinclair and others, Wellington to Sir George Gipps in GBPP 1842 (No.569) 10 Feb 131 [cf 10 Sep report a. above]]

>  The distance of these allotments from the town is under the prescribed limit given by Sir George Gipps; ... <i>(Jul 19, 1841,GBPP,132</i>)

>  From the extreme value of allotments with water frontages for commercial people, the [Auckland] waterside was subdivided into very small allotments ... but two of the best of these [allotments], bordering on a fresh-water creek, which runs down through the valley, have been selected by Government officers. <i>(Jul 19, 1841,GBPP,134</i>) [NOTES: D. Sinclair to Lord John Russell in GBPP 1842 (No.569)]

>  The town sections were chosen on the 21st April. The suburban sections will not be chosen for a month or two yet; and before the rural allotments are selected, a still longer period will elapse. <i>(May 5, 1848,Letters from Otago,14</i>) [NOTES: in 1878 Letters from Otago 1848-1849]

>  The allotments in this division ... <i>(1849,Notes on Otago</i>) [NOTES: [1971 G.J.Griffiths notes: used frequently in early days, probably because many sections were allotted, but uncommon today] (Griffiths Collection)]

>  <b>BUDDLE</b> [Ngaruawahia] has been surveyed ... and a large Town laid out in one acre allotments, ... the streets being named after Maori tupunas (ancestors) and living chiefs. <i>(1860,Maori King Movement,15</i>)

>  ... the oak and the elm ... are conspicuous in our streets and suburban allotments. <i>(1894,TrNZI,26/621</i>)

## alluvial
 
 <i>n.</i> gold-bearing alluvial soil or alluvium, any gold-bearing alluvial soil. [goldmining]

>  <b>BAINES</b> The latter place ... having rich alluvial, or poor men's diggings, was a very bank with an ever-ready balance in favour of the clever and the muscular. <i>(1874,Edward Crewe,265</i>)

>  Al-lu'.vi-al (&abreve.l-l&umacr.'.v&ibreve.-al), n. Gold-bearing alluvial soil. Alluvial in this sense is in general use in Australia, where the term alluvion is never heard. <i>(1898,Merriam-Webster Third International,2017</i>)

>  <b>MORRIS</b> Alluvial, the common term in Australia and New Zealand for gold-bearing alluvial soil. The word is also used adjectivally as in England. <i>(1898,Austral English,2</i>)

>  With respect to the soils of Auckland, nowhere in New Zealand within such short distances is there such a diversity in the quality - a distance of half a mile often makes all the difference between rich alluvial and barren pipe-clay. <i>(1902,Settler's Handbook of NZ, The,2</i>)

>  <b>PFAFF</b> We ... did not locate payable gold. As a matter of fact we passed the gold zone as it is now recognised, as the alluvial only extends a certain distance back from the coast line. <i>(1914,Diggers' Story, The,42</i>)

>  <b>BLYTH</b> Black Sand: These deposits occur all along the West Coast of the South Island and are composed of black iron and other metals sometimes carrying gold in alluvials also. <i>(1935,Gold Mining Year Book,11</i>) [NOTES: Glossary]

>  <b>'VICTORIAN MINER'</b> A great many specimens [of gold] are found in the alluvial. <i>(1862,Rambles through Goldfields</i>) [NOTES: Otago Times]

>  <b>LEE</b> Men were out shovelling and turning over alluvials. <i>(1944,Shining with the Shiner,30</i>) [NOTES: check 60??]

>  <b>YATE</b> Kahikatea (Treniperus, or Dacrydium excelsum) - This tree only flourishes in low, swampy, or alluvial soils; ... <i>(1835,New Zealand (various authors/titles),39</i>)



 
 <i>adj.</i> [AND [spec. use of alluvial pertaining to alluvium] of or pertaining to gold-bearing alluvium: .. 1892 In the alluvial days, alluvial ground: also alluvial claim, digger, miner rush working.] [a. In the combinations: alluvial action; alluvial claim [AND 1859 - HWO: poss. keep separate.]; alluvial deposit; alluvial digging; alluvial diggings [AND 1858 - HWO: poss. keep separate.]; alluvial ground; alluvial miner [AND 1858.]; alluvial mining [AND 1867 - HWO: poss. keep separate.]; alluvial producer; alluvial prospecting; alluvial rush [AND 1871.]; alluvial soil; alluvial washing; alluvial workings [AND 1865 - HWO: poss. keep separate.].]

>  <b>SUTHERLAND</b> Smooth, worn gold lay together with coarse, `shotty' and rough-sided pieces [47] which could not have travelled far through alluvial action. <i>(1953,Golden Bush, The,46</i>) [NOTES: 1963 ed.]

>  <b>EVANS</b> On December 10, 1871, Messrs. F. Fraser and James Sinclair applied for a licence to build a water race ... terminating in an extended alluvial claim. ... An alluvial claim is one acre. An extended alluvial claim exceeds one acre up to five acres. <i>(1962,Waikaka Saga,14</i>) [NOTES: 1871 in 1962 Evans Waikaka Saga 10 Dec]

>  .. the old Glengyle and Hit or Miss alluvial claims <i>(1903,TrNZI,35/403</i>)

>  The Canterbury and Nelson regulations classified mining claims as alluvial, river, creek, beach, or quartz, and the shape and size of claims differed in each case. ... [footnote follows] The term `alluvial' was misapplied since river, creek and beach claims were strictly alluvial claims, too. The `alluvial claim' was defined in the regulations as a claim not in the watercourse or stream-bed of a creek or river. `Ordinary claim' was a more suitable term than `alluvial claim' and `ordinary' was adopted in the revised regulations of 1867-8. <i>(1962,West Coast Gold Rushes,247</i>) [NOTES: May]

>  <b>WOOD</b> The ordinary alluvial claims were at first 24 ft square, then 30 ft square, but in 1866 the size was increased to 100 ft square. <i>(1970,Gold Trails of Otago,7</i>)

>  ... the alluvial miners thought they were deling with superficial alluvial deposits; they called theconglomerates the `granite wash'. Deeper workings showed that the beds dipped steeply, and the gold continued downwards, the conglomerates were then called `deep leads', probably in the belief that they resembled the deep leads of Victoria. <i>(1933,NZ Journal of Science & Technology,14/272</i>)

>  <b>BLYTH</b> Alluvial Deposit: Gravel, sand, clay, etc.; deposited at a distance from its source by the agency of water. <i>(1935,Gold Mining Year Book,11</i>) [NOTES: Glossary]

>  Alluvial deposit: A deposit formed by material washed down or transported by water. <i>(1974,25 Years of Care</i>) [NOTES: Glossary of Mining Terms]

>  The progress of lode-mining is in some way analogous to that of alluvial digging ... <i>(1931,NZ Journal of Science & Technology,12/159</i>)

>  ... country, too, which is expected to produce alluvial diggings of great wealth. <i>(Oct 27, 1867,Trip to the Thames Gold-fields,29</i>) [NOTES: 1978 ed.]

>  ... no permanent mining industry of any extent has been established in New Zealand. ... The alluvial diggings in Otago and West Coast can hardly with any propriety be termed mines, or those who work them, miners. ... For the sake of convenience, I shall, in the following essayobserve a distinction between the alluvial digger, by whom I understand one who gathers gold from the surface and from later deposits only, and the alluvial miner, that is, one who works the deeper deposits, or deep placer mines as they are called. <i>(1969,AJHR,D-6</i>) [NOTES: R.H. Eyton Settlement of Goldmining Pop of NZ]

>  It is therefore not merely the existence of auriferous rocks at the surface ... which determines the ... richness of alluvial diggings... <i>(1870,TrNZI,2/371</i>) [NOTES: Hector Mining]

>  <b>WIERZBICKA</b> This is the case in all alluvial diggings, and very soon unless new ground is found at the creek, diggings in New Zealand will be done up and they will have to form companies to work the quartz. Already they have two quartz mines on the Nevis which are paying tolerably well, and ... there will be many more soon. <i>(Jan 29, 1873,Wilson Family,156</i>) [NOTES: G.M. Wilson Diary in 1973 Wilson Family]

>  <b>BAINES</b> The latter place ... having rich alluvial, or poor men's diggings, was a very bank with an ever-ready balance in favour of the clever and the muscular. <i>(1874,Edward Crewe,265</i>)

>  <b>KNOX</b> Some of the mines are wholly alluvial, or placer, diggings, others are wholly quartz-mines, which are called reefs in New Zealand and Australia, ... <i>(1888,Boy Travellers,215</i>)

>  <b>HARPER</b> True, there are still many working the alluvial ground [on the West Coast], but those making more than `tucker' are few, and it is many years since `a rise' of importance has been made by anyone. <i>(1896,Pioneer Work in the Alps of NZ,30</i>)

>  ... no permanent mining industry of any extent has been established in New Zealand. ... The alluvial diggings in Otago and West Coast can hardly with any propriety be termed mines, or those who work them, miners. ... For the sake of convenience, I shall, in the following essay observe a distinction between the alluvial digger, by whom I understand one who gathers gold from the surface and from later deposits only, and the alluvial miner, that is, one who works the deeper deposits, or deep placer mines as they are called. <i>(1869,AJHR,D-6</i>) [NOTES: R.H. Eyton Settlement of Goldmining Pop.of NZ]

>  Mr. Skey found the new mineral ... in a sample of heavy black sand, reported as saved by alluvial miners in Barn Bay ... <i>(1891,NZ Journal of Science & Technology,1/160</i>)

>  ... aided by ... an old alluvial miner - the locality was diligently prospected, and one or two `colours' are said to have been found in the wash in the bed of the Mangaone Stream ... <i>(1893,TrNZI,25/354</i>)

>  .. the alluvial miners thought they were dealing with superficial alluvial deposits; they called the conglomerates the `granite wash'. Deeper workings showed that the beds dipped steeply, and the gold continued downwards, the conglomerates were then called `deep leads', probably in the belief that they resembled the deep leads of Victoria. <i>(1933,NZ Journal of Science & Technology,14/272</i>)

>  It was not in gold-saving but in the variety of methods and contrivances used to seek out and work the auriferous deposits that the skills of the alluvial miner were displayed. <i>(1962,West Coast Gold Rushes,239</i>) [NOTES: 1967 ed. May]

>  <b>WOOD</b> So you must find ... what the alluvial miners termed the `bottom'. This can be either a `false bottom' - usually a coarse bed of gravel, but sometimes a cemented layer; or a `true bottom', which is usually the basal surface of the whole deposit. <i>(1970,Gold Trails of Otago,83</i>)

>  <b>BROWN</b> Although work was available in the quartz mines, many preferred the independent life of the alluvial miner, and claims were soon pegged off at the Doughboy ... <i>(1976,Difficult Country,63</i>)

>  [three classes of mining at the Dunstan : River beach workings, tunnelling and] alluvial mining. <i>(1863,AJHR,D-6/17</i>)

>  The outcome was a brilliant series of discoveries which added a new facet to alluvial mining. <i>(1962,West Coast Gold Rushes,169</i>) [NOTES: (1962) ed. May]

>  <b>VEITCH</b> It was thought best to appoint Jackson Keddell, who was already in charge of the Gold Escort, asCommissioner, ... <i>(1976,Clyde on the Dunstan,13</i>)

>  GOLD explorer Spectrum Resources Ltd is fast becoming an alluvial producer in addition to its Monowai mine project north of Thames. <i>(Oct 9, 1987,National Business Review,1</i>)

>  In 1889 alluvial prospecting was renewed in the hope that detrital cassiterite would be found in payable quantities ... <i>(1934,NZ Journal of Science & Technology,15/348</i>)

>  R.A. Loughman's The First Gold Discoveries in New Zealand (1906) is a handy summary of the alluvial rushes in Westland and elsewhere. <i>(1962,West Coast Gold Rushes,533</i>) [NOTES: 1962 ed. May]

>  <b>OLLIVANT</b> ... the miners have long since advanced from the early stage of alluvial washing to the crushing process. <i>(1879,Hinemoa,63</i>)

>  <b>HAY</b> Here, the mining is all quartz ... There is no alluvial washing to enable one topan out one's dust, ... <i>(1882,Brighter Britain,2/274</i>) [NOTES: [? purely attrib use of aj =`the washing of alluvial'; ??or is this a spec. concrete use of WASHING, =the result of geological formation, =ALLUVIAL DEPOSIT]]

>  <b>PFAFF</b> These [black sand] leads yielded a richer return ... than probably any other alluvial workings in New Zealand ... <i>(1914,Diggers' Story, The,29</i>)

>  <b>COWAN</b> In the sixties there were ten thousand diggers on the alluvial workings here, chiefly on the ocean beach where these heavy dark sands were combed for the gold dust. <i>(1926,Travel in NZ,Vol II South Island : 90</i>)

>  <b>HOWARD</b> ... the return from alluvial workings was far too slight to meet the cost of lode development. <i>(1940,Rakiura,304</i>)

>  Workings [at Mount Ida, Otago, in 1871] were for the most part alluvial, the distinctive process used being `paddocking'. [fn] Paddocking was the systematic sluicing of large areas of flat ground from which the surface layers were first removed to reveal the `wash-dirt' beneath. <i>(Apr 1953,NZ Geographer,29</i>) [NOTES: Kibblewhite]

>  <b>NOLAN</b> Up the Blue Creek track today you can still see piles of stones from the old alluvial workings, and pieces of mining equipment. <i>(1976,Historic Gold Trails of Nelson & Marlborough,72</i>)

## alluvial gold
 
 <i>n.</i> (See [alluvial adj. and noun](../A#alluvial-adj.-and-noun))

>  <b>MUNDY</b> Collingwood is one of the oldest mining fields, but very little alluvial was being worked when we went there. [???here = `alluvial gold'] <i>(1948,There's Gold in Them Hills,30</i>)

>  Most of the diggers on prospecting alluvial claims work for themselves, or with a mate or two, ... <i>(1894,NZ Official Yearbook,369</i>) [NOTES: E. Tregear: Labour in NZ]

>  <b>D'AVIGDOR</b> At Kumara, which is now the centre of alluvial gold mining on the West Coast, hundreds of wooden canals ... bring water down ... <i>(1888,Antipodean Notes,123</i>)

>  How is gold formed into specks and nuggets? and [I will] also dispute the theory that alluvial gold was not derived from reefs. <i>(1890,TrNZI,22/400</i>)

>  ... there is a great difference in the standard of fineness between alluvial and reef gold. <i>(1898,TrNZI,30/499</i>)

>  Alluvial gold has originally been derived from quartz reefs in the adjacent country. <i>(1902,Brett's Colonists Guide,1030</i>)

>  <b>HENDERSON</b> ALLUVIAL GOLD: Gold obtained on the surface by means of water sluicing. <i>(1961,Free Lance,185</i>) [NOTES: c1900-1920 A Teamster's Glossary in 1961 Henderson Friends In Chains 185]

>  <b>CARR</b> Gold is found, both alluvial and quartz <i>(1913,Country Work,10</i>)

>  Near Deep Creek ... miners in search of alluvial gold have sunk through 80 ft. of gravel in the stream bed before reaching solid rock. <i>(1918,NZ Journal of Science & Technology,1/11</i>)

>  <b>BLYTH</b> Nugget: Alluvial gold in a solid lump. <i>(1935,Gold Mining Year Book,11</i>) [NOTES: Glossary]

>  Equally naturally, the decline in alluvial gold production after 1870 increased the supply of labour available for other industries, ... <i>(1936,Agricultural Organization in NZ,6</i>)

>  The original source of all Otago's alluvial gold is the quartz reefs that cut through the mica schists ... <i>(1970,Gold Trails of Otago,81</i>)



## alluvial goldfield
 
 <i>n.</i>

>  <b>TINNE</b> [It] is to be ... the great alluvial gold-field of this island. It has not been `prospected' yet, from the reluctance of the natives to mining encroachments ... <i>(1873,Wonderland of the Antipodes,43</i>)

>  The South Island was free from Maori wars ... Here in the Greenstone Land, the chief element of adventure and romance was supplied by the successive `rushes' to the great alluvial goldfields in Otago and on the West Coast. <i>(1926,Travels in NZ,viii (Vol II South Island)</i>)

>  Thus Kumara, the best alluvial goldfield on the West Coast, was not discovered till 1876 ... <i>(1931,NZ Journal of Science & Technology,12/159</i>)

>  Neither the development of ground-sluicing nor the tunnel claims made Ross and Donoghue's distinctive. The absorbing topic at `the Ballarat of Westland' was the deep leads. These produced the most imposing mining works on the West Coast and gave Ross a permanence unusual for an alluvial goldfield. <i>(May 1962,West Coast Gold Rushes,188</i>) [NOTES: 1962 ed. May]



## alluvial goldmining
 
 <i>n.</i>

>  They tell us that in the palmy ... days of alluvial gold-mining, it was no uncommon sight to see over fifteen hundred miners ... <i>(May 1904,NZ Illustrated Magazine,103</i>)

>  <b>PFAFF</b> For Westland proper, alluvial gold mining has been its main pursuit. <i>(1914,Diggers' Story, The,87</i>)

>  It must be remembered that gold is now chiefly obtained by quartz-mining. The placer-claim is no longer the chief source of the gold-supply, and consequently the supply is no longer subject to the vicissitudes invariably connected with alluvial-gold mining. <i>(1903,TrNZI,35/129</i>)

## alpine
 
 <i>adj.</i> [AND only alpine ash a eucalypt; OED(S none of those below mentioned.] As a distinguishing epithet in the names of plants and animals, see astelia, avens, blueberry, broom, bush-flax, carpha, club-moss, creeping coprosma, donatia, epacris, eyebright, fern - filmy fern, hard fern, rue-leaved fern, shield-fern, tangle-fern, umbrella-fern; fescue tussock, foxglove; grass - bent grass, holy-grass, oat-grass, rice-grass, whorl-grass; marigold; pine, celery; quillwort, ribbonwood; rush, speargrass, sun-dew, toatoa, totara, tutu. 2. As a distinguishing epithet in the names of animals, see galaxias, parrot, kiwi, weta.



## alpine celery pine
 
 <i>n.</i> [aka mountain toatoa]

>  As we ascend the character of the vegetation changes, the mountain beech [fn <i>Fagus cliffortioides</i>.] replaces that of lower altitudes, and the undergrowth consists largely of the alpine celery pine [fn <i>Phyllocladus alpinus</i>.], an almost unique member of the order <i>Coniferae</i>, with the [188] customary needle-like leaves replaced by flattened leaf-like branches, ... <i>(1900,Canterbury Old & New,186</i>)



## Alpine fault

## alpine parakeet
 
 <i>n.</i> = orange-fronted parakeet

>  <i>Orange-fronted parakeet Cyanoramphus malherbi</i> (SOUANCE', 1857) ... Since European settlement the orange-fronted parakeet has never been as common as other New Zealand parakeets. ... OTHER NAME: <i>Alpine parakeet. ... Probably now in the South Island only. </i> <i>(1985,Reader's Digest Book of NZ Birds,247</i>)

>  Renowned ornithologist Walter Buller added to the confusion by claiming to discover the orange-fronted parakeet in the Southern Alps in 1869, naming it <i>Platycercus alpinus</i>, the alpine parakeet. <i>(May 2001,NZ Geographic,51/10</i>)

## alpine totara

>  <i>Podocarpus hallii</i> alpine totara (ii) <i>P. nivalis </i> <i>(1925,Duncan & Davies' Catalogue</i>) [NOTES: (And)]

>  <b>KIRK</b> <i>Podocarpus nivalis</i> alpine totara <i>(1889,Forest Flora</i>) [NOTES: (And)]

>  <b>COCKAYNE</b> <i>Podocarpus nivalis </i>alpine totara <i>(1919,NZ Plants & Their Story</i>) [NOTES: (And)]

>  <b>LAING & BLACKWELL</b> <i>Podocarpus nivalis </i>(The Alpine Totara). <i>(1940,Plants of NZ,68</i>)

>  <i>alpine totara n </i>: a dense New Zealand shrub (<i>Podocarpus nivalis</i>) often low and widely spreading with leaves closely and irregularly arranged <i>(1961,Merriam-Webster Third International,62</i>)

>  Native species to New Zealand include the alpine totara (Podocarpus nivalis) which tends to be a low-growing shrub reaching 3m in height and spreading out to some 1.8m. <i>(Oct 1, 2010,Waikato Times,15</i>)

>  Amongst ornamental trees and shrubs that grow rampant in the neighbourhood are two pines, a phyllacladus and the alpine totara (a shrub resembling the English yew, and equally beautiful), several senecios, the large-blossomed ribbonwood, and so on. <i>(Feb 6, 1896,Otago Witness,53</i>)

## alpine weta
 
 <i>n.</i> <i>Pharmacus montanus</i>, aka jumping spider, Mount Cook flea

>  I now have to refer to insects, or rather to one that I met with - the great Alpine Weta - whose bite is perhaps more dangerous than that of any other insect found in the forest .. It will be exhibited before the Natural History Society, Wellington, and named 'Deinacrida Broughi.'" <i>(Dec 18, 1895,Colonist (Nelson),2</i>)

>  The scientific name of the insect is <i>P. montanus.</i> It appears to be variously referred to as `Mount Cook Flea', `Jumping Spider' etc., but as the insect is allied to the weta, a more correct name would be <i>Alpine Weta. </i> <i>(1935,NZ Alpine Journal,6/22/172</i>) [NOTES: (Dumbleton)]



## Alps
 
 <i>n.</i> In New Zealand, the Southern Alps, New Zealand Alps; later Southern Alps.

>  <b>FORSTER</b> We saw the Snow lying on the tops of some of the New-Zeeland Alps, whilst we enjoyed the verdure & mild air below, at no great distance from them. <i>(Apr 18, 1773,Resolution Journal of J.R. Forster, The,2/260</i>)

>  alp n. mountain-peak, esp. in NZ (the Southern Alps) <i>(1986,NZ Pocket Oxford Dictionary,19</i>) [NOTES: 1982 Collins Concise Eng. Dict. 30; 1985Collins NZ Compact Dict. 13]

>  ... then he may be qualified to speak of the scenery of this country - especially if he has added to his sketchbook the great chain of the southern Alps, which I have lately seen in all their wintry grandeur, stretching in an almost unbroken line from north to south for more than three hundred miles. <i>(Oct 16, 1849,Daily Southern Cross,4</i>)

>  For the first time you here cross the Mackenzie stream, which is a small boggy creek at this point, and then ascend to the saddle by a zig-zag track, and in a few minutes rest on its summit. The Mackenzie Country then bursts suddenly on your view, with its mountains, rivers, and lakes, and the snow capped Alps in the back-ground. <i>(Dec 10, 1870,Timaru Herald,2</i>)

>  The immortal Cook tells us, that the Alps of the southern island exceed those of the northern in height, and are covered with eternal snow. <i>(Aug 1, 1840,New Zealand Gazette & Wellington Spectator,2</i>)

## amalgamated claim
 
 <i>n.</i>

>  AMALGAMATED CLAIM: claims adjoining one another thrown together temporarily or permanently tofacilitate working. <i>(May 1962,West Coast Gold Rushes,526</i>) [NOTES: 1967 ed.]

>  The striking of the lead in Wildridge and party's and Sheehan and party's amalgamated claim has caused quite a stir at Half-Ounce. <i>(Sep 8, 1870,Grey River Argus,2</i>)

>  James Rea, being sworn, stated he and his mates held an amalgamated claim of ten men's ground. <i>(Oct 6, 1880,West Coast Times,2</i>)

>  Section 64 - Amalgamation of claims. As section 112. Amendment: After "amalgamate the same," the following is added: "but always so that the amalgamated claim or holding shall not exceed in area the maximum area prescribed for claims or licensed holdings, respectively as the case may be." <i>(Nov 26, 1891,Otago Witness,14</i>)

>  GREY RIVER CONSOLIDATED DREDGING COMPANY. .. Mr A. J. Park moved, and Mr Thomson seconded-"That the directors be authorised to sell the amalgamated claim of the company to the new company, when formed, for GBP5000, and to accept fully paid-up shares in the new company in lieu of cash." - Carried unanimously. <i>(Dec 25, 1901,Otago Witness,21</i>)

>  As the Maoris have just been mentioned, an anecdote concerning them may not be out of place at even the tail end of the letter. Seven of these men held an amalgamated claim some distance up the river, and it is an extremely valuable one. <i>(Feb 14, 1863,Nelson Examiner and New Zealand Chronicle,3</i>)

## Amazing Grimaldi Brothers
 
 <i>n.</i> a drinking game

>  Other well-known [drinking] games are Hokonui Swindle, Bottles, Fluffy Ducks, Bunnies are Hovering, Cardinal Huff, All Blacks, Matches, and The Amazing Grimaldi Brothers. All these games involve either a complicated intellectual exercise, tongue twisters, mathematical calculations or acrobatics in a confinedspace; High Candelabras for example, involves intoxicated individuals attempting to traverse the four walls of a room without touching the floor. <i>(Mar 13, 1988,Dominion Sunday Times,16</i>)



## amber fluid
 
 <i>n.</i> (informal) (also amber liquid, amber elliptical) beer. [31 [rest of dateline lost] Sinkin' the old amber fluid has been right in the spotlight lately, what with plans to let 18-year-olds front the bars legally.]

## amberjack

>  <b>FALLA</b> RED-TAILED TROPIC BIRD <i> Phaethon rubricauda</i>. Other names: Amokura, Strawtail. <i>(1966,Birds of New Zealand,59</i>)

## ambrite
 
 <i>n.</i>

>  <b>HOCHSTETTER</b> `Although originating probably from a coniferous tree related to the Kauri pine, it nevertheless has been erroneously taken for Kauri gum.'-[Footnote]: `It is sufficiently characterised to deserve a special name; but it comes so near to real amber that it deserves the name of Ambrite.' [This is the earliest use of the word. Morris] <i>(1867,New Zealand (Hochstetter),799</i>) [NOTES: [Morris check]]

>  <b>MORRIS</b> Ambrite, (generally called ambrit),... Mineral [from amber + ite, mineral formative, `O.E.D.'], a fossil resinfound in masses amidst lignite coals in various parts of New Zealand. Some identify it with the resin of Dammara australis, generally called kauri gum (q.v.). <i>(1898,Austral English,2</i>)

>  ambrite n. yellowish-grey fossil resin found in NZ. [AMBER] <i>(1986,NZ Pocket Oxford Dictionary</i>) [NOTES: nf. 1982 Collins Concise Eng. Dict.]

>  On examining SOME OF THE LIGNITES OF OTAGO, we find large trunks of trees, masses of leaves and twigs, matted together, and containing lumps of fossil resins known as ambrite, the whole forming a very excellent fuel for local purposes. <i>(Apr 13, 1888,Otago Witness,36</i>)

>  A piece of resinous substance lying on a shelf in the office of the Undersecretary for Mines attracted the attention of a Post representative to-day. The pressman was curious to know whence it came, and was informed that it was a lump of ambrite which had been found in one of the South Island coalmines. <i>(Aug 9, 1906,Evening Post,8</i>)

## American
 
 <i>adj.</i> Used of things thought to be North Amer. or of NorthAmer. origin. American alley [nf OED2], a children'smarble game; American broom; American blight; American pegged boots, ??hob-nailed, ??spriggedboots; American shirt; American stove; American wagon, also waggon [AND 1855; poss.OED wagon 6.; see W3 1e. and illus.], ??a drag; ??a kind of stage-coach with seating inside and on top and drawn by four horses; American wire, [??Omit: ??merely descr. use.], barbed wire. [See also blackberry, brooklime, groundsel, marshpennywort, nightshade, waterwort.]

>  Thomas Redpath.. Has now the followingarticles for sale.. American Axes. <i>(Jan 26, 1861,Otago Witness,1</i>)

>  The following goods have now arrived ex"Pirate"... American Cooking StovesComplete. <i>(Feb 2, 1861,Otago Witness,3</i>)

>  <b>KEITH & MAIN</b> Marbles were the currency of childhood and there were an astonishing number of names for them, some referring to the material or colour, others to value or function in the game. There were agates, aggies, American alleys, blood alleys, bomb-squashers, bonies, bonsers, bottlies, bull's eyes, bum-squashers, cats-eye, [sic] chinkies, connies, duckies, eggies, glassies, Greeks, milkies, mollies, peelies, peewees, pisswees, pretties, Scotties, smokies, stinkies, stonies, stripies, tom-bobblers and whities. The march of technology added ball bearings - ballies or steelies - which were unbeatable when it came to smashing an opponent's miserable glassie. World War II [roman] brought `clayeys' often imperfectly spherical they were pathetic terracotta substitutes for real marbles. <i>(1984,NZ Yesterdays,288</i>)

>  [Zosterops] subsists almost entirely [in Wellington orchards and gardens] on the destructive little Aphis known as `American Blight'. <i>(1869,TrNZI,1/III/10</i>) [NOTES: Buller Ornithology]

>  <b>SUTTON-SMITH</b> Marbles had a language of its own. There were names for the marbles, the games, and all the various aspects of the play. There were terms for marbles such as `taw' or `shooter', referring to the marbles used to fire with;`dubs', `dates', `stakes', `dukes', or `changers', referring to the marbles put down to be fired at. Then there werethe terms referring to particular kinds of marbles: for example, agates, aggies, aggotties, American alleys, blood alleys, bomb-squashers, bonies, bonsers, bottlies, bull's eyes, bum-squashers, cat's eyes, chinkies, connies, duckies, eggies, glassies, Greeks, milkies, molly-bars, mulley-bars [mullibars: large marbles], peelies, peewees, piss-wees, pretties, Scotties, Scottish alleys, smokies, stinkies, stonies, stripies, tom-bobblers, white alleys, and in more recent years ball-bearings, chippies, plastics, and woodies. <i>(1972,Folkgames of Children,174</i>)



 
 <i>n.</i> short for American axe. (See [American axe](../A#american-axe))



## American axe
 
 <i>n.</i> A double-edged axe, a double-bitted (or poss. a broad-bladed) axe [one with two cutting edges on the blade (??broad-bladed axe often called broad-axe and used spec. for splitting and shaping logs for e.g. sleepers). Also yankee axe, American.]

>  <b>WAKEFIELD</b> The Colonist's attention should be directed to the following articles, with which he must provide himselfaccording to his means and his intentions. For clearing and cultivating timbered land:- American axes, of various sizes. ... Common English axes. (The natives can use these best).... <i>(1848,Handbook for New Zealand, The,447</i>)

>  All should take some carpenter's tools, especially a good axe (American ones are best) ... <i>(Sep 13, 1849,Letters from Otago,38</i>) [NOTES: 1978 ed.]

>  <b>HURSTHOUSE</b> The American axe and cross-cut saw ... <i>(1849,Account of Settlement of New Plymouth, An,96</i>)

>  Harry and I went down to the Beach House and opened our tool cask ...[sic] we bought a large camp oven ..., 3 English-American axes 7/- each and billhooks 5/-. <i>(Oct 22, 1853,Richmond-Atkinson Papers,1/136</i>) [NOTES: (A.S. Atkinson)]

>  [stores list] 6 American axes/ 6 American hatchets <i>(May 11, 1856,Richmond-Atkinson Papers,1/264</i>) [NOTES: (J Morgan)]

>  <b>HURSTHOUSE</b> From their skill [...] in using the American axe, clever management of fire, and knowledge of what is termed`burning off' they are found to be capital hands in the clearing for cultivation of bush land. <i>(1853,New Zealand (various authors/titles),14</i>) [NOTES: The Emigration Field of 1851in 1963 NZ Geog Apr 56 (Hargreaves)]

>  <b>HURSTHOUSE</b> ... the trees are thrown with the x [sic] cut saw and American axe. <i>(1857,New Zealand (various authors/titles),2/331</i>)

>  <b>PAUL</b> ... the American axe ... <i>(1857,Letters from Canterbury, NZ,71</i>)

>  He answered `I do not consent [to slay my child],' and forthwith took an American axe, and cut off a hand and a foot. <i>(1864,AJHR,E-8</i>) [NOTES: T.H. Smith Memorandum: enclosure written to Dr. Nesbitt, Resident Magistrate, Rotoruaby Rawiri in 1937 Babbage Hauhauism 73]

>  <b>BAINES</b> Do not suppose that those ugly dull wedges, sometimes to be seen in a hardware-shop in England, and labelled American axes, though of British manufacture, are equal to the Yankee article. They are no more so than the mis-shapen stick affixed is to an American hickory axe-handle... One day, since my return to this worn-out old country, I happened to meet a friend who some time previous having heard of the superiority of the Yankee axe, had bought one of these British imitations, and finding the tool a poor one, had jumped to the conclusion that, `after all,' there was no axe equal to the old-fashioned one in use in England, and was considerably `riled' at my intense derision and scorn of that conservative weapon; indeed, so hot did we get in our dispute that I offered to chop against the best woodman he could produce, his man armed with the medieval tool, whilst I was to use an American which I happen still to have ... <i>(1874,Education,172</i>)

>  <b>WILLIAMS</b> Toki, n. axe; adze; ... Toki poke; American axe. <i>(1892,Dictionary of the NZ Language,203</i>)

>  <b>WILLIAMS</b> Poke, American axe. [so 1957/289] <i>(1917,Dictionary of the NZ Language,336</i>)

>  ... american axe ... <i>(May 1869,Guide to Farming in NZ,19</i>)

>  The best tools for bush-felling are an American axe, about five pounds weight, and a light slash-hook with athin edge. <i>(1902,Brett's Colonists Guide,19</i>)

>  <b>FANNING</b> If the mangle's mainspring has a fit, don't restore it to consciousness with a tomahawk or the business end of an American axe. <i>(1910,Players and Slayers,5</i>)

>  AMERICAN METHODS... Their first great achievement was the American axe. Most persons can remember what a clumsy, heavy ineffective tool the old English axe was. .. It is curious too, to notice that the very same people who invented the American axe should also invent the most scientific method known for grinding axes. <i>(Feb 25, 1908,Evening Post,3</i>)

>  ... the majority of English farmers would rather hold the plough for a week than use the American axe for a day. <i>(1850,Australian & NZ Gazette,60</i>) [NOTES: in 1963 NZ Geographer Apr 30 Nov 48 (Hargreaves)]



## American invasion
 
 <i>n.</i> The influx of US servicemen from the Pacific theatre to New Zealand (mainly Auckland and Wellington) [WW2. Hist. c1942-1944. Also, by extension, the influences of this influx.]

>  <b>CAMERON</b> The `invasion' of New Zealand by friendly American troops ... were obvious signs of a revolution in the making. <i>(1965,New Zealand (various authors/titles),2</i>)

>  <b>SIMPSON</b> ... that happening ... which we may now refer to as `The American Invasion' of 1942. <i>(1945,If You'd Care to Know,75</i>)

>  <b>MULGAN</b> When the American `invasion' came, we found this [British] `yoke' and [imperial] `exploitation' idea wascommonly held [by the Americans]. <i>(1958,Making of a New Zealander, The,144</i>)

>  Instead of employing such literary calls the American Invasion demands a "rock and rye," or a "stone fence," or a "high ball," or " a "Ramzen cooler," or some other insinuating beverage whose mysteries are a secret known only to the holy of holies of the American bar. <i>(Jul 23, 1901,Star, The,4</i>)



## American waggon
 
 <i>n.</i>

>  Mr Teakle's transport.. was a fourwheeler called an "American waggon". It had a long body which could take three seats placed crosswise, but if only two or three passengers were aboard just one seat was enough, leaving a good deal of spare room behind. <i>(1976,Elizabeth of Lavington,109</i>)

 <i>abbr.</i> anhydrous milk fat

>  AMF is pure milkfat, with virtually all the water removed... "Someone in Mexico is getting AMF at 70 per cent of the cost to import it from New Zealand to the US, about US$1000 a tonne," said American dairy farmer John Bunting... <i>(Oct 22, 2003,Dominion Post,C4</i>)

 <i>abbr.</i> Associate Minister of Foreign Affairs and Trade [ORIGIN: acronym]

>  No such miracles for the ever bouncy associate Minister of Foreign Affairs and Trade Marian Hobbs. She looks just the same as she did last year and even jokes at the acronym that goes with her portfolio - AMFAT. <i>(Feb 22, 2003,Dominion Post</i>)



## amo
 
 <i>n.</i> (as pl.) upright supports of the lower ends of the maihi on the front gable of a house. [ORIGIN: Maori]

>  <b>TREGEAR</b> In front of the verandah of a house, short perpendicular slabs (amo), often carved or painted, supported the barge-boards (maihi) of the gable, and formed very striking and highly embellished adornments. <i>(1904,Maori Race, The,273</i>)

>  <b>55/151</b> amo, upright supports at lower ends of the maihi in front of a house. <i>(1946,Journal of the Polynesian Society,ANDERSON</i>) [NOTES: Andersen Maori Words in English JPS]

>  In the 1880's the amo and maihi passed into the hands of Archdeacon Henry Williams who in 1913 presented them to the Dominion Museum. <i>(May 2005,Kokiri Paetae,11</i>)



 
<b>2</b> <i>n.</i> litter, bier [ORIGIN: Maori, [Ma. amo (see note n1.) a litter, Ma. /amo/ W.8 Amo ... 4 ... Litter, bier.]]

>  <b>WILY & MAUNSELL</b> As they were about to place him in the amo (the native palanquin) he seized my hand and said, ... <i>(Mar 17, 1846,Robert Maunsell,94</i>) [NOTES: 1938 ed.]

>  On the third day six ... natives came out of the bush, made an `amo' (stretcher) and carried me on their shoulders from Mimi to my house in New Plymouth. <i>(Mar 27, 1899,Richmond-Atkinson Papers,2/622</i>) [NOTES: 1960 ed. Robert Parris]



## amoko
 
 <i>n.</i> (See [moko](../M#moko))



## amokura
 
 <i>n.</i> Phaethon rubricauda roseotincta , fam. Phaethontidae, a white ternlike seabird of the subtropical region, occas. straggling to NZ. The two red elongated central tail feathers were highly prized by the Maori (see cit. 1946). Also called red-tailed tropic-bird.

>  <b>TREGEAR</b> The most valued kinds [of feathers] were tail-feathers of the <i> huia </i> ... and the long red tail-feathers of the <i> amokura<i> (Tropic bird: Phaethon rubricauda). <i>(1904,Maori Race, The,243</i>)

>  <b>ANDERSON</b> <i> amokura</i>, an ocean bird (Phaethon rubricauda), red-tailed tropic-bird: the two long springly feathers of the tail used as plume in head-dress. Amokura is the Maori name: in the Pacific it is tavake, and the Maori has that word, with the usual letter-change, tawake, in an ancient whakatauki (proverb or sententious saying), but that name has otherwise been forgotten in New Zealand, where the bird is found only after storms in the Pacific, and then usually dead on the shore. <i>(1946,Journal of the Polynesian Society,55/151</i>) [NOTES: Andersen Maori Words in English]

>  <b>SUTHERLAND</b> Nevertheless the Maori bartered ... feathers of many hues - mainly those of the heron, huia, tropic bird (<i> amokura</i>), ... and the kaka <i>(1941,Numismatic History of NZ,13</i>)

>  <b>HENDERSON</b> He wore ... in his hair plumes of the <i> amokura</i>. <i>(1948,Taina,134</i>)

>  74. <i> Phaethon rubricauda roseotincta</i> Mathews, 1926 <i> Red-tailed Tropic Bird </i> Breeding Kermadec, Norfolk and Lord Howe Islands ... <i>(1970,Annotated Checklist of Birds of NZ,30</i>) [NOTES: 1980 ed.]

>  <i> Red-tailed Tropic Bird Phaethon rubricauda roseotincta </i> MATHEWS, 1926 ... OTHER NAMES: <i> Bo'sun bird, amokura</i>. ... The Maoris of the North Cape region prized the long tail streamers of the red-tailed tropic bird. After easterly gales it is said they systematically searched the coast for these birds. <i>(1985,Reader's Digest Book of NZ Birds,108</i>)

>  The early Wesleyan and English Church Mission missionaries, who arrived in New Zealand in the early years of the century, found the Ngapuhi tribe of the far north in possession of the red tail feathers of the tropic bird, which were then much prized decorating the heads of chiefs on festive days. The bird is known to the Natives under the name of Amokura. <i>(Jun 3, 1899,Ashburton Guardian,2</i>)

>  Dr Newman says the amokura is really a native of the tropical islands of the Pacific, and is only a visitor to New Zealand, though accounted by some to be a native. It was on account of its rarity that the Maori chiefs of the old days prized the feathers of the amokura, especially as the feathers are tinged with the sacred kura, or red colour. <i>(Jan 15, 1907,Poverty Bay Herald,4</i>)



## amp (up)
 
 <i>v.</i>

>  The word "amp" is also commonly used among the younger generation to express positive feelings, for example, "the crowd was really amped up at the rugby match". <i>(Aug 19, 2000,Listener (New Zealand Listener),48</i>)

>  At school when you're doing the kapa haka and stuff, that kind of amps you up to think, 'Hey, man, like my ancestors, they were like hard-going men, we were tough'. <i>(Aug 15, 2004,Sunday Star Times,C3</i>)

>  As we drove into Hamilton searching for our next home, we began to get amped for the week's line up of schools. <i>(Oct 2005,Tearaway,14</i>)

>  Thus, <i>Broken English</i> amps up its various cultural conflicts and images, piling them up to create complex resonances. <i>(Sep 21, 1996,Listener (New Zealand Listener),44</i>)



## amuri
 
<b>1</b> <i>n.</i> (Rural) [Ma] a New Zealand-bred cultivar of oats.

>  Following its release in 1967 Amuri, the most recent variety of oat bred at the DSIR crop research division Lincoln, has become popular as a greenfeed variety and as an early-maturing chaff oat ... <i>(Apr 1969,NZ Journal of Agriculture,56</i>)

>  Our work has demonstrated that Nui ryegrass is severely affected when sown under the biennial ryegrass Paroa, less so under Tama, and most successful with Rahu ryecorn and Amuri oats. <i>(Mar 1979,NZ Journal of Agriculture,58</i>)

## ana
 
 <i>n.</i> a cave or shelter [Rare or idiolectic. The explorer Brunner uses it for a cave, rock shelter or declivity used as a sleeping place during his Nelson-Westland travels in the late 1840s; not recorded elsewhere in English contexts.]

>  <b>NICHOLAS</b> A cave Hana <i>(1817,New Zealand (various authors/titles),2/330</i>)

>  <b>POLACK</b> We were arrested in our progress half way by a cavern, (hana,) [a's-acute] which stopt our further progress. <i>(1838,New Zealand  (Polack),2/134</i>)

>  <b>BRUNNER</b> Towards the afternoon we had occasional showers, but we kept pushing on, and just before dusk reached a large ana, or hole in the rocks, where we put up for the night. <i>Ibid.</i>. 1847 Feb 19. 267 Proceeded on our journey, and once again reached our ana, or former sleeping place, ... <i>Ibid.</i>. 267 23d. ... About one mile of fearful walking to an ana, where we found dry, but most uncomfortable lodgings on an uneven surface of granite rock. <i>(Jan 26, 1847,Diary</i>) [NOTES: Brunner (1848) in 1959 Taylor Early Travellers 26 Jan 266]



## anabranch
 
 <i>n.</i> [Obs.]

>  <b>WHITWORTH</b> I camped for the night on the bank of the ana-branch I have mentioned. <i>(1870,Martin's Bay Settlement,35</i>)

>  An'.a-branch (&abreve.n'.&adot.-br&adot.nch), n. [Contr. fr. anastomosing branch.] A branch of a river which re-enters the main stream or loses itself in sandy soil. See billabong, 2. <i>(1898,Merriam-Webster Third International,2017</i>)

>  <b>PYKE</b> Here the river runs almost level with the land, coursing around tiny islands, and flowing in many ana-branches, or `back-waters', as they are termed. <i>(1873,Wild Will Enderby,3/iii/81</i>) [NOTES: (1889,1974)]

## ancestral connection
 
 <i>n.</i> an iwi's historic connection to part of the foreshore

>  The compromise deal is that the Crown will be enshrined as owner as owner of the beaches, but tribes may lay claim to certain usage rights, based on ancestral connection. <i>(May 1, 2004,Listener (New Zealand Listener),14</i>)

>  Dr Cullen says the term "customary title" is confusing and has been widely misunderstood. The Waitangi Tribunal says the term is a "misnomer" when the foreshore and seabed will still be owned by the Crown with Maori rights lessened. It has been replaced by "ancestral connection"... Ancestral connections, where recognised, will bring Maori more ability to participate in decision making.... Ancestral connections: iwi connections to a particular part of the foreshore and seabed may give a guardianship role. Proved connection will give iwi more say in the management of the area. <i>(Apr 8, 2004,Dominion Post,B5</i>)

## anchorman
 
 <i>n.</i> [Students'.]

>  A game called Anchorman played by the students at one of the University of Otago's Halls of Residence, requires participants to consume six jugs of ale in four hours without being sick. The reward is an Anchorman's badge. ... Although it has been banned by college authorities ... [it is] regarded as a way of sorting out `Men' from the `chunder bunnies' or `coma kids'. To become an Anchorman is to be like an anchor which stands firm even when completely submerged. <i>(Mar 13, 1988,Dominion Sunday Times,16</i>)



## anchovy
 
 <i>n.</i> (a) Scientific name: <i>Engraulis australis</i>. (b) Suggested commercial name[s]: Anchovy. (c) Other common names: the following names of related Clupeids and Engraulid fish are sometimes applied to anchovy - Herring, Sprat, Sardine, Pilchard, although the true Herring of Europeanwaters (<i>Clupea harengus</i>), does not occur in New Zealand. (d) Principal localities where taken: Hauraki Gulf, Gisborne, Tasman Bay, Marlborough Sounds. A small sea-fish (see esp. 1970 cit.).

>  <b>HUTTON</b> 99. ENGRAULIS ENCRASICHOLUS. L. Anchovy <i>(1872,Fishes,62</i>)

>  <b>SHERRIN</b> ANCHOVY (<i>Engraulis encrasicholus</i>). ... It was found at the mouth of the River Thames, and called by the Natives there korowhawha. <i>(1886,Handbook Fishes of NZ,9</i>)

>  ENGRAULIS ANTIPODUM [<i>australis 1922 <i>Ibid.</i> V/94</i>] (Gunther[u-dieresis]). <i>Anchovy; Kokowhawha</i>. ... Distribution: Recorded from Thames, Bay of Plenty, and Hauraki Gulf. <i>(Jun 1921,NZ Journal of Science & Technology,IV/118</i>) [NOTES: Phillipps Notes on the Edible Fishes]

>  There appear to be three species of fish belonging to the family Clupeidae in New Zealand waters, viz., <i>Engraulis antipodum</i>, or anchovy; <i>Sardinia neopilchardus</i>, or pilchard, and <i>Harengula antipoda</i>, or sprat. <i>(1926,TrNZI,56/314</i>) [NOTES: Young and Thomson]

>  <b>PHILLIPPS</b> <i>Engraulis australis</i> (Shaw). ANCHOVY; KOROWHAWHA. <i>(1927,Bibliography of NZ Fishes,11</i>) [NOTES: 1971 ed]

>  <i>Engraulis australis</i>: Anchovy. <i>(Jan 1936,Handbook for New Zealand, The,71</i>) [NOTES: ANZAAS Conf. Jan. 1937]

>  <b>PHILLIPPS</b> ANCHOVY OR KOROWHAWHA <i>Engraulis australis</i> (Shaw) ... The anchovy or korowhawha of the Maori is not well known to many European fishermen. It is very like the European species both in general appearance and in structure. <i>(1940,Fishes NZ,5</i>)

>  <b>GRAHAM</b> Anchovy <i>Austranchovia australis</i> (White) (Syn. <i>Engraulis encrasicholus var. antipodum</i> Gunther) <i>(1955,NZ Fishes: An Identification Guide,398</i>) [NOTES: Whitley Namelist in 1956 Graham]

>  <b>WILLIAMS</b> <i>Korowhawha, Engraulis antipodum; anchovy. </i> <i>(1957,Dictionary of the Maori Language,146</i>)

>  <b>McLINTOCK</b> anchovy .. .. kokowhawha, korowhawha .. .. <i>Engraulis australis</i> <i>(1966,Encyclopaedia of New Zealand, An,3/705</i>)

>  <b>AYLING</b> ANCHOVY <i>Engraulis australis</i> ... Anchovies are the common baitfish around most of the North Island and are also common in the north and west of the South Island. <i>(1982,Collins Guide,105</i>) [NOTES: 1984 ed.]

>  <b>PAUL</b> <i>Anchovy Engraulis australis</i> Restricted to New Zealand and Australia, but similar species occur elsewhere. <i>(1986,NZ Fishes: An Identification Guide,41</i>)

>  <b>PAUL</b> <i>(1987,Fishes: A Bibliography</i>) [NOTES: [index to common names 283-293]]

>  [species name] anchovy .. [scientific name] <i>Engraulis australis</i> .. [other name(s)] - <i>(Jul 23, 1986,Codes for Species in NZ Waters</i>) [NOTES: Fisheries Research Div]



 
<b>2</b> <i>n.</i> (See [pilchard](../P#pilchard))

>  <b>SORENSON</b> PILCHARD (a) Scientific name: <i>Sardinops neopilchardus</i>.. (b) Suggested commercial name[s]:Pilchard; Sardine. (c) Other common names: Sardine; Sprat; Anchovy; Herring. (d) Principal localities where taken: Auckland, Gisborne, Tasman Bay, Marlborough Sounds. <i>(1970,Nomenclature of NZ Fish,40</i>)

## angelfish
 
 <i>n.</i> [Usu. black angelfish]

>  <b>TAYLOR</b> Fam. <i>Squatinorajae</i>, (Papaki.) - This extraordinary-looking fish abounds on the New Zealandshores, it is very repulsive in its appearance, and has little claim to the title of angel fish; the natives, however, eat it, drying it the same as the shark. <i>(1855,Te Ika a Maui,626</i>)

>  <b>DOOGUE & MORELAND</b> BLACK ANGELFISH ... <i>Other names: Parma microlepis</i>; white-ear (Australia). <i>(1966,Sea Anglers' Guide,254</i>)

>  <b>MORELAND</b> BLACK ANGELFISH [<i>Parma microlepis</i>] ... called white-ear in Australia. <i>(1967,Marine Fishes,38</i>)

>  <b>AYLING</b> BLACK ANGELFISH (Black damselfish) <i>Parma alboscapularis</i> ... Until recently this fish was thought to be identical to the Australian white-ear <i>Parma microlepis</i> ... As their name suggests the adults of both sexes are a uniform black in colour with a prominent white ear spot that can be turned [239] off and on like a signal light . <i>(1982,Collins Guide,238</i>)

>  <b>PAUL</b> <i>Black angelfish Parma alboscapularis</i> Family Pomacentridae. Apparently restricted to New Zealand ... <i>(1986,NZ Fishes: An Identification Guide,98</i>)

>  <b>PAUL et al.</b> angelfish, black 1545 <i>(1987,Fishes: A Bibliography</i>)

>  Species include red moki, black angelfish, Lord Howe coral fish, stingrays and sinister-looking moray eels ... <i>(Oct 28, 2003,Sunday Star Times,C11</i>)

## angiangi
 
 <i>n.</i> <i>Coprosma repens</i>, a shrub [aka taupata, cf hangehange]

>  <b>BULLER</b> Around the base ... there is a dense growth of stunted angiangi (<i>Coprosma lucida</i>) looking very fresh and green. <i>(1888,Birds of New Zealand,2/151</i>)

>  Where the slopes are broken ... the following shrubby trees grow abundantly: Kohepiro (<i>Angelica rosaefolia</i>), angiangi (<i>Coprosma baueri</i>) ... <i>(1930,NZ Journal of Science & Technology,11/152</i>) [NOTES: Fraser]

>  At the mouth of the river are the Angiangi trees (Coprosma), where the Arawa canoe landed sixteen generations ago and her two stone anchors, Tokaparore and Tuterangiharuru, are still visible. <i>(Dec 16, 1902,Hawera & Normanby Star,2</i>)



 
<b>2</b> <i>n.</i> <i>Usnea barbata</i>, a soft white lichen used as a wrapper or absorbent

>  The material used among the Tuhow natives ... as a menstruating diaper is a variety of moss (generic term <i>rimurimu</i>) known as <i>kohukohu</i> or <i>angiangi</i>. It is probably <i>Hypnum clandestinus. </i> <i>(1905,TrNZI,37/90</i>)

>  <b>BROOKER et al.</b> <i>Lembophyllum clandestinum</i> (H.f. & W.) Lindb. Maori names: <i>Angiangi, kohukohu </i>... Goldie listed `angiangi' as <i>Hypnum clandestinum</i>, a `fern', but <i>H. clandestinum</i> is a moss, now known as <i>Lembophyllum clandestinum</i>. [Williams identifies angiangi as <i>Usnea barbata</i>, a lichen, and it is probable that the word was applied generally to soft absorbent mosses and lichens.] <i>(1981,NZ Medicinal Plants,25</i>)



## angle post

>  Angle post: Posts placed where the fence changes direction. Need extra support by stays or tie-backs. <i>(1982,MAF Agricultural Glossary,29</i>)

>  <b>MAF</b> Stays: Support for strainer or angle post. <i>(1982,MAF Agricultural Glossary,29</i>)

>  <b>FISK</b> Once the end strainers and angle posts are all in you are ready to run out a wire to get a line. <i>(1996,Practical Smallfarming,13</i>)

>  Fence posts on a bend (angle posts) or in a hollow (dip posts) need special attention in fence construction ... <i>(Feb 10, 2002,RD1.com,Farm Management Ag Fact</i>)

>  <b>RIDDOLLS</b> All angle posts should be fitted with a stay or breast block. <i>(1958,Farm Engineering,418</i>)

>  The lecturer said he had seen straining posts put in solid rocky country that would not require footing, but it was always wise to foot a strainer and an angle post, as it would be a difficult matter to repair such neglect should the strainer or angle post come up once the fence had been erected. <i>(Oct 28, 1921,Ohinemuri Gazette,4</i>)

>  A FENCING DISPUTE. .. On the Tuesday before they left the job defendant said the wires were not tight enough, and they strained them, and he told then [sic] to start putting the battens on. At defendant's request they gave the angle post a lean. <i>(Sep 30, 1910,Poverty Bay Herald,2</i>)

>  Describing himself as a half-caste, Judge Jurie said that there were many times he was tempted to publicly reject allegations of Maori bias. ... `But it is saying that Anglos can hear Maori cases and they will never be accused of bias ... It assumes that Anglo are fair but Maori are not and that hurt me a great deal.' <i>(Mar 14, 1989,Dominion,2</i>)



## Anglo-colonial
 
 <i>n.</i>

>  <b>SICLAIR</b> The Royal Colonial Institute in 1868, the Imperial Federation League in 1884, the British Empire League a decade later, all numbered among their most energetic founders colonials, Canadians and Australians as well as New Zealanders, living in England. The reaction against the anti-imperialist views of the extreme free traders had been traced to the protests of these `Anglo-colonials against the withdrawal of imperial troops from New Zealand in 1868. <i>(1959,History of New Zealand,213</i>)



## Anglo-Maori
 
 <i>n.</i> a person of mixed British and Maori race or descent. [Anglo-New Zealander includes generalisation, persons, race of] (See [cf. Anglo-New Zealander](../A#anglo-new-zealander))

>  <b>GREENWOOD</b> Half-castes ... presenting too often the painful picture of an Anglo-Maori, with all the tastes and habits and feelings of the savage. <i>(1850,Journey to Taupo,36</i>)

>  <b>HAY</b> The Anglo-Maori may be said to be divided into two distinct classes - those whose education has been chiefly ... English, and those who have `tumbled up' in the kainga, in all respects like the Maori. <i>(1882,Brighter Britain,2/137</i>)

>  <b>MEAD</b> Wallis's [the Wesleyan missionary at Waingaroa] children were fine specimens of Anglo-Maoris. <i>(1966,Richard Taylor,169</i>) [NOTES: [?Taylor's diary c.3 Feb 1851]]

>  <b>McNEISH</b> Wiremu wore a grass skirt over his trousers and a broken panama hat in the style of the Anglo-Maoris of the time; ... <i>(1970,Mackenzie,12</i>)

>  "Tenakoe," said Mac, with a true Maori roll of his tongue. "Tenakoe," responded the aboriginal. "And what might be your name?" queried Mac, falling into the dialect of the Anglo-Maori. <i>(Feb 16, 1907,Observer (Auckland),4</i>)



 
 <i>adj.</i>

>  <b>TAYLOR</b> Another similar Anglo-Maori force was sent from Wanganui to Opotiki, to act in conjunction with others to punish the murderers of Mr. Volkner. <i>(1868,Past and Present of NZ,165</i>)

>  <b>HAY</b> Half-breeds, or Anglo-Maori men and women, form no inconsiderable section of the native community. <i>(1882,Brighter Britain,2/135</i>)

>  <b>SORRENSON</b> By contrast, with his Anglo-Maori ancestry, Buck continued to believe in the amalgamation bymiscegenation of the races. <i>(1986,Na To Hoa Aroha,1/17</i>)

>  <b>TREGEAR</b> Such Anglo-Maori words as paraoa, flour; honi, honey; karahi, glass, are easily recognizable ... <i>(1885,Aryan Maori,95</i>)

>  <b>SORRENSON</b> The myth survived with the Maori to bolster pride in the Anglo-Maori achievement. <i>(1979,Maori Origins and Migrations,84</i>)

>  There are many words which require correction, and it would be well if the native name is to be retained, that the proper word should be used. A dusky savage would absolutely laugh at the Anglo-Maori caricature of his forcible and expressive language. <i>(Nov 3, 1870,Tuapeka Times,6</i>)

## Anglo-Maori War
 
 <i>n.</i> . [aka Anglo-Maori wars (pl) and New Zealand War/s]

>  <b>BINNEY et al.</b> The inland Anglican Station which had been founded at Te Whaiti in 1847, had been abandoned with the onset of the Anglo-Maori wars ... <i>(1979,Mihaia,15</i>)

>  <b>MCINTYRE</b> During the Anglo-Maori Wars of the 1860s he was willing to recognise the King Movement. <i>(1980,Journal of Henry Sewell,1/16</i>)

>  <b>BOYD</b> By peaceful coexistence with settlers, they hoped to avoid the consequences of the Anglo-Maori Wars in Taranaki and the Waikato, namely conquest and land confiscation. <i>(1984,City of the Plains,6</i>)

>  Mr Chapman also raised the interesting question of whether or not the tiriti was abrogated by the 1845-72Anglo-Maori wars. If these wars were a case of foreign invasion, then the members of those tribes which which resisted did lose the rights conferred by the tiriti. <i>(Aug 9, 1986,Listener (New Zealand Listener),11</i>)

>  <b>SCOTT</b> ... Sir William Russell Russell ... who had escaped active service in the Anglo-Maori wars by selling his commission to become a Hawkes Bay squatter. <i>(1987,Seven Lives on Salt River,85</i>)

>  The Anglo-Maori war of the Sixties began in 1860 over a disputed land purchase at Waitara in Taranaki. <i>(1890,Soldier's View of Empire, A,128</i>) [NOTES: SINCLAIR (1982) in c1890 Bodell A Soldier's View of Empire 128]

>  The Maori warrior has never shown himself a contemptible foe. From the commencement of the Anglo-Maori war, when Heki surprised and overcame the Settlement of Kororarika, [sic] on how many occasions have they shown that they are a warlike nation, their warriors bold and active, and their chiefs full of cunning; with the heart to dare, the head to plan, and the hand to do the boldest achievements. <i>(Feb 9, 1865,Evening Post,2</i>)

>  'One of the oldest Natives in the Colony was in town to-day, on tribal business, - Te Potae, who claims to be at least a centennarian [sic]. .. In the Anglo-Maori war he was loyal to the Queen and acted as guide to the British troops at the time of the Gate Pah fight and was awarded the war medal. <i>(Apr 7, 1899,Bay of Plenty Times,2</i>)

>  <b>HILL</b> His real motivation was to avoid sparking off an Anglo-Maori war. <i>(1986,Policing the Colonial Frontier,1/154</i>)



## Anglo-New Zealand
 
 <i>adj.</i>

>  This article cannot be closed without pointing out, for the satisfaction of the Anglo-New Zealand public, advantages which New Zealand naturally possesses, as antidotes to some of the evils which Mr Hewet has experienced. <i>(Feb 3, 1844,New Zealand Gazette & Wellington Spectator,3</i>)

>  The Manager of the Colonial Bank and Captain William Ashby represented the Anglo New Zealand commercial community at the banquet given by the London Chamber of Commerce to Sir Charles Warren on Wednesday evening. <i>(Dec 8, 1885,Star, The,3</i>)

>  Amongst the Anglo-New Zealand magnates who have suffered most seriously from the depression in the colony and depreciation in the value of land is, says the London correspondent of the Dunedin Star, the Hon Thomas Russell, C.M.G. <i>(Oct 4, 1888,Marlborough Express,2</i>)

>  Mr Reeves, speaking on behalf of the High Commissioner, tendered a welcome to Sir Joseph and Lady Ward .. The present Conference was by no means the least important of them all. He hoped that it would be guided by that cool, deliberate statesmanship in keeping with the dignity, the strength and the traditions of the Empire, and on behalf of the Anglo-New Zealand community he wished Sir Joseph every success. <i>(Sep 14, 1909,Star, The,3</i>)

 <i>n.</i> [New Zealanders of British birth, also New Zealanders residing in Britain. Rare. Obs.]

It seemed to the Anglo-New Zealander as if the United States was fast being hurried on to a state of industrial revolution, and to a direct and general trial of strength between the unions and the trusts[.] <i>(Aug 29, 1903,New Zealand Free Lance,3</i>)

## animated straw/animated twig
 
 <i>n.</i> old names for the stick insect [Also <i>Acanthoderus horridus</i>, and other spp.]

>  <b>SATCHELL</b> ... he threw himself down in a thick clump of tea tree, and was soon absorbed in watching a battle between two full-grown `animated straws', ... <i>Ibid.</i> fn4. p.215 `"Animated straws"'.Sometimes the Straw or Teatree Straw Insect, usually the New Zealand Stick Insect or (formerly) Walking Stick Insect also known as the Teatree Jack, <i>Acanthodem?us horridus</i> of Hudson [An Elementary Manual of NZ Entomology 110 has only Family PHASMIDAE <i>Acanthoder?us horridus</i> ... The curious Stick insects ... [HWO note]] (1892). Thin, segmented, attenuated fine legs; capable of camouflage by colour change. Satchell refers to this at the end of the paragraph. <i>(1902,Land of the Lost,33</i>) [NOTES: ed. Smithyman 1971]

>  <b>CARTER</b> The scrub or bush is covered with a species of Mantidae, called by the settlers animated twigs. <i>(1862,Life in NZ,429</i>)

>  Tawhiao replied to Purukutu's request as follows: - "If you see a mantis [Roo is the word used in the Maori language, and the insect is sometimes called 'the animated straw' by bushmen] upon a manuka bush, pick it off carefully. But if you see a reptile crawling across the road, you are to kill it and eat it [.] " <i>(May 20, 1873,Daily Southern Cross,3</i>)

>  An animated straw scaled a ti-tree stem, then turned a wise face to examine the twain who had disturbed him. <i>(Dec 7, 1907,New Zealand Free Lance,35</i>)



## anise
 
 <i>n.</i> <i>A. rosaefolia koheriki</i> W.125 <i>A. rosaefolia.] [Transf. use of </i>anise,<i> an aromatic plant.] Angelica </i>(??esp. <i>A. montana</i>) and/or <i>Anistome</i> spp. [aniseed. ??Mainly SI.] (See [aniseed](../A#aniseed))

>  <b>HOCKEN</b> The anise plant, so valuable as pasture for sheep and cattle, abounds over all the land we traversed. <i>(Aug 31, 1844,Contributions to the Early History of New Zealand,267</i>) [NOTES: Colonel W. Wakefield Letter in 1898 Hocken]

>  <b>WAKEFIELD</b> In some parts of the district, however, and especially in the plain of the Wairarapa, an indigenous anise-plant abounds among the natural pasture, adding greatly to the quality and flavour of the flesh of cattle which have fed on it. <i>Ibid.</i> 246. The <i>Anise</i> plant is very abundant in all the pastoral districts near Nelson; and ... it much improves the ... meat fed on land where it grows. <i>Ibid.</i> 305. [Otago land] covered with herbage, ... and anise of larger growth than any I had previously seen. <i>(1848,Handbook for New Zealand, The,148</i>)

>  Fine soil ... on the high banks: mixture of grass, fern, small tutu and anise. <i>(Jan 3, 1849,Torlesse Papers,46</i>)

>  This country abounds with what is called the armis plant, in all appearance something like parsley, the sheep and other cattle are very fond of it. <i>(Oct 27, 1849,Letters from Otago,42</i>) [NOTES: 1978 ed. spg ?error <i>armis</i>]

>  <b>MONRO</b> ... we descended towards the [Canterbury] plain by gentle slopes beautifully grassed, and well stocked with anise and sowthistle. <i>(Jul 20, 1844,Not Here, Not Now</i>) [NOTES: Nelson Examiner July 20 et seq.) in 1898 Hocken Contributions 235]

>  <b>ADAMS</b> ... thickly spread with the Anis plant of which horses are particularly fond ... <i>(1853,Spring in the Canterbury Settlement, A,64</i>) [NOTES: Griffiths Collection) [also 57]

>  <b>EARP</b> About 200,000 acres consist of open land covered with grass, anise, fern, flax, and tohitohi. <i>Ibid.</i> 191 [quoting Col. Wakefield's letter to Secretary of New Zealand Company pp.9, 10] The anise plant, so valuable as pasture for sheep and cattle, abounds on all the [Otago] land we traversed. <i>(1853,New Zealand (various authors/titles),113</i>)

>  <b>BARRINGTON</b> Caught a hawk which we cooked with a little anniss. [ed fn] Some plant of the carrot family, probably a species of <i>Anisotome</i>, with aromatic leaves and seeds; or it might be a closely related Angelica. <i>(Jan 9, 1864,Diary,397</i>) [NOTES: in 1959 Taylor Early Travellers 9 Jan 397 [spg anniss]]

>  <b>COCKAYNE</b> <i>(1919,NZ Plants</i>)

>  <b>BUTLER</b> On the saddle there was coarse grass which was in full seed; and therefore very nourishing for the horse; also abundance of anise and sow-thistle, of which they are extravagantly fond, ... <i>(1872,Erewhon,ch iii</i>)

>  <b>BEATTIE</b> Anise grew the thickest I ever saw at Mount Durden ... <i>(1939,First White Boy Born in Otago,50</i>)

>  <b>BEATTIE</b> [W.H.S. Roberts writes] Instead of conserving the native grasses ... the early settlers burned them off ... so that steep slopes that in the 'fifties were covered with rich `blue grass' and anise are now quite bare. <i>(1947,Early Runholding in Otago,152</i>)

>  <b>BEATTIE</b> Spear-grass is an aromatic plant, but the scent came principally from musk and anise. The latter was usuallyknown as aniseed. <i>(1947,Pioneer Recollections,6</i>) [NOTES: 1956 ed.]

>  <b>DUNCAN & DAVIES</b> [rosaefolia] (And) <i>(1925,NZ Native Trees</i>)



 
 <i>n.</i> [Both in ref to <i>Angelica montana</i> and <i>A gingidum</i> and in unspecified usage. Both variants aka kohepiro and kohiriki.]

>  <b>COCKAYNE</b> native anise [Angelica montana] (And) <i>(1910,NZ Plants</i>)

>  Sheep and rabbits have greatly lessened, though they have not quite exterminated, the native anise (Angelica <i>Gingidum</i>), which I remember seeing growing abundantly in the Molyneux Valley near the mouth of the Waitahuna Stream. <i>(Jan 1922,NZ Journal of Science & Technology,4/282</i>) [NOTES: Bathgate]

>  <b>COCKAYNE</b> native anise [<i>Angelica montana</i>] (And) <i>(1924,Cultivation of NZ Plants, The</i>)

>  In its natural state, the face of the country is covered with silver and blue tussock, barley and oat grass, the anise plant, tutu, and here and there cabbage trees and flax in luxuriant growth. <i>(Feb 23, 1878,Otago Witness,20</i>)

>  Country that I have seen entirely denuded of every kind of grass except the tussock appears to have quite recovered its original condition, even the anise plant, which was supposed to be quite destroyed, is making its appearance again. <i>(Apr 17, 1890,Otago Witness,7</i>)

>  On many a rocky cliff, for example where the Anderson's Bay road winds round just beyond Musselburgh, grew patches of angelica, more commonly known as " anise," but these have either been eaten out by cattle or have been choked out by hardier plants. <i>(Dec 26, 1900,Otago Witness,70</i>)

>  The appearance and colour of this tract of country indicated that it was partly, though not purely grassy. I believe it will be found to be covered with short fern principally, with a considerable sprinkling of grass, anise, flax, etc. <i>(Mar 18, 1908,Otago Witness,55</i>)



## aniseed
 
 <i>n.</i> . [Mainly <i>Angelica</i> spp., esp. <i>Angelica montana</i> (formerly <i>A. gingidum</i>) but occas. poss.<i> Anistome</i>; in NZ, spp. ??Mainly SI. [Transf. and extended use of <i>aniseed</i>, the seed of anise.]formerly used as wild fodder plants (mainly wild angelica).] (See [anise](../A#anise))

>  <b>TUCKETT</b> Like the narrow plains of the Waikauroa, it grows only the wiry tree grass or something [225] similar, and <i>Phormium tenax</i>, no aniseed, perennial groundsel, or milk thistle, and very little couchgrass, ... [Otago] ... the prevailing species of trees are remo [rimu], totara, and tall manuka, all indicative of inferior soil.... The bush-like woods on the east side are of white birch, likewise no recommendation of the soil, but the chief objection to the district is, that the prairie soil affords scarcely any food for cattle ... it grows only the wiry tree grass [tussock] ... and <i>Phormium tenax</i> [flax], no aniseed, perennial groundsel, or milk thistle, and very little couch grass. <i>(May 24, 1844,Diary</i>) [NOTES: Tuckett Diary in 1898 Hocken Contributions 24 May 223-224]

>  <b>TAYLOR</b> [Angelica montana] (And) <i>(1855,Te Ika a Maui</i>)

>  <b>THOMSON</b> An indigenous aniseed grows in many parts greatly improving the flesh of the animals feeding on it. <i>(1867,Twelve Years in Canterbury,17</i>) [NOTES: quoting Chambers Papers for the People]

>  <b>KIRK</b> [Angelica Gingidium] (And) <i>(1899,Students' Flora</i>)

>  <b>CHEESEMAN</b> [<i>Angelica Gingidium</i>, Hook f.] From Taupo southward to Otago; once very abundant, but as it is everywhere eaten by stock it has become scarce in many districts. <i>(1906,Manual NZ Flora,223</i>)

>  <b>LAING AND BLACKWELL</b> Angelica Gingidum (The Native Aniseed.) ... The whole plant is highly aromatic. It is greedily eaten bysheep, and though originally very abundant, is often almost exterminated by them in accessible places. The plant is known to shepherds and runholders as <i>Aniseed. </i> <i>(1907,Plants of NZ,322</i>)

>  <b>COCKAYNE</b> ... and the aniseed (<i>Angelica Gingidium</i>). <i>(1910,NZ Plants,80</i>)

>  <b>REISCHEK</b> The vegetation [of Campbell Island] is tussock mixed with cotton-plants, aniseed, veronica, and other plants, ... <i>(1930,Yesterdays in Maoriland,281</i>) [NOTES: 1933 ed.]

>  The following species appear to merit thorough testing as to their nutritive quality ...: <i>Deyeuxia avenoides</i>, plume-grass (<i>Dichelachne crinita</i>) ... native oat-grass (<i>Trisetum</i> spp. ... aniseed (<i>Angelica montana</i>), and some species of <i>Anistome.</i> <i>(1938,NZ Journal of Science & Technology,20/233A</i>)

>  <b>LAING AND BLACKWELL</b> <i>Angelica montana</i> (Maori Anise). ... Both islands ... [as 1907 cit. above] The plant is known to shepherd and runholders as <i>Aniseed. </i> <i>(1940,Plants of NZ,342</i>)

>  <b>BEATTIE</b> Spear-grass is an aromatic plant, but the scent came principally from musk and anise. The latter was usually known as aniseed. <i>(1947,Pioneer Recollections,6</i>) [NOTES: 1956 ed.]

>  <b>MACFARLANE</b> The outstanding plant in these preserves and the one that disappeared as the deer came along was aniseed. We do know that it was common everywhere once and gave a bright sheen, when in flower, to the whole of NorthCanterbury; but sheep appeared to relish it and, being a soft plant with no defence, it has vanished except on isolated bluffs and crannies. <i>(1954,Te Raka,40</i>)



## aniseed ball
 
 <i>n.</i> a sweet

>  <b>LAWLOR</b> Other sweets current at the time were `Cupid's Whispers', ... sen-sens, cable twist, ... butter balls, acid drops ... aniseed balls ... mint rock. [check Lawlor's diaries] <i>(Jan 15, 1903,Diary</i>) [NOTES: Lawlor Diary in 1977 Dominion 17 Sep 15 Jan 7]



## anisotome
 
 <i>n.</i> usu. <i>Anistome aromatica</i> [nf OED2, W3] (See [cf. anise](../A#anise),[aniseed](../A#aniseed))

>  ...there is much to enjoy up close as the sink holes are festooned with summer flowers - buttercups, daisies, euphrasias and anisotomes, and the promise of gentians, closed against the rain. <i>(Mar 13, 2004,Dominion Post,E17</i>)

>  <b>McLEOD</b> Gentians and edelweiss abound [in the Canterbury high country] and the delicate anisotome that sheep feed on to extinction. <i>(1980,Tussock Ranges,62</i>)

>  <b>LAING AND BLACKWELL</b> [various spp.] <i>Anistome aromatica (The Aromatic Anistome)</i>. Very aromatic, 4 in.-12 in. high. ... Abundant in mountainous districts, <i>and usually known as mountain aniseed</i>. ([Formerly] Ligusticum aromaticum). ... This is a remarkable shingle-slip plant ... <i>(1940,Plants of NZ,334-336</i>)

>  <b>COCKAYNE</b> <i>Anistome aromatica</i> Common anistome <i>(1919,NZ Plants</i>) [NOTES: AND]



## ankle-biter
 
 <i>n.</i> toddler, small child

>  Suppose a friend says to me, `You know that drongo who was on the chain with me at the works last summer? Just a little joker he was, remember? A real serious ankle-biter. Hailed from somewhere down the Wire Wrapper. A right useless bastard. Couldn't see the road to the dunny if it had red flags on it. Well, it seems that he's shacked up with some pluty Remmers sheila and she's about to drop her bundle.' ... A non-New Zealander will probably require a translation, ... My friend is communicating to me the information that a slow-witted, incompetent person of diminutive stature, who was reared in the Wairarapa district and worked for a time in the killing gang of an abattoir, is now cohabiting with a wealthy woman from the affluent Auckland suburb of Remuera. This woman is soon to give birth. <i>(Jan 1989,Pacific Way,10</i>) [NOTES: Sharp New Zildish]

>  But does the radio dial offer any more relief for bored ankle-biters, crecheniks and fractious parents today? <i>(May 4, 1992,Listener (New Zealand Listener),87</i>)

>  Damned ankle-biters! Must fly. <i>(Sep 5, 1992,Dominion,10</i>)

>  <b>McGILL</b> ankle-biter small child. NZA. <i>(1988,Dictionary of Kiwi Slang, A,10</i>)

>  [She] was seen recently hoofing it about the village watering holes.. with what looked awfully like an ankle-biter wielded to her hip. <i>(Sep 1992,Metro,22</i>)
