## Ark in the Park
 
 <i>n.</i> (Environment) a restoration project to provide a protected open sanctuary for indigenous plants and animals in the Waitakere Ranges.

>  Many community restoration groups such as Ark in the Park (based in the Waitakere Ranges) are endeavouring to return this mistletoe to the Auckland region by reducing possum numbers in the ranges. <i>(Jan 11, 2007,NZ Herald,A7</i>)

>  The Waitakere Branch of Forest and Bird has a partnership with the Auckland Regional Council to create the Ark in the Park open sanctuary in the Cascades Kauri Park. <i>(Aug 2003,Auckland Naturally,3</i>)

>  The Ark in the Park project has reached the stage where species can be reintroduced to the Waitakere Ranges ... <i>(2004,Canopy,3,Spring ed.</i>)

>  'Ark in the Park' in the Waitakere Ranges .. are among the projects the fund supports. <i>(Dec 20, 2005,Scoop</i>)

>  The hihi transfer is part of the Ark in the Park project, a partnership between the Waitakere Branch of Forest and Bird and the Auckland Regional Council, supported by DOC, Auckland Zoo Conservation Fund, Waitakere City Council, ASB Community Trust and Waitakere and Portage Licensing Trusts. <i>(Jun 2007,Conservation Action,55</i>)

## Armed Constabulary
 
 <i>n.</i> Hist. Also lower case initials. [nf W3.] The armed police, reconstituted as a civilian force after 1886, formed in 1867 under the Armed Constabulary Ordinance as the first national police force (?called earlier the armed police force) orig. to combat hostile Maoris, and to keep civil order, until the civil (unarmed) police was established in 1886 under the Police Force Act. (See [A.C](../A#a.c))

>  According to the booklet published by the Taupo District Museum "<i>the armed Constabulary carried out three main functions in the short time of its existence. It was a military force largely concerned with frontier protection; it was a police force concerned with peace-keeping and crime control and it was a public works force concerned with building roads, bridges and telegraph lines.</i>" <i>(Jun 2002,NZ Memories,No. 36: 58</i>)

>  Some particulars were given of Mr Kirk's meritorious career.. as a member if that splendid force, the Armed Constabulary. <i>(Aug 27, 1904,NZ Observer,5</i>)

>  Nearly all of the whares occupied by the Armed Constabulary were blown down. <i>(Mar 27, 1885,Wairarapa Daily,2</i>)

>  <b>BARBER</b> Maori, Colonial and Imperial participation in the New Zealand wars of the 1840s and 1860s, the development of militia and the birth of a vigorous volunteer force, together with the creation of the Armed Constabulary, marked the first 50 year's of the army's life. <i>(1984,Red Coat to Jungle Green,8</i>)

>  [Inscription] This monument, to the memory of the Officers and Men of the Armed Constabulary And of the Patea Field Force.. Is erected by the Officers and Man of the A.C. Field Force. <i>(Jan 1, 1887,Auckland Weekly News,7</i>)

>  <b>HARSANT</b> Here we would take potatoes and matches,and then catch a small variety of carpcalled 'morihana'... Their Maori name,'morihana', was a translation of"Morrison.' A captain, I believe, in theArmed Constabulary days, Morrison hadintroduced the fish to Taupo and LakeRotorua <i>(1979,They Called Me Te Maari,56</i>)

>  <b>LAMBERT</b> ... the discovery of Waikare-iti by Major Goring, of the Armed Constabulary Force. <i>(1936,Pioneering Reminiscences of Old Wairoa,153</i>)

>  Whitmore has gone as a Volunteer to the front. I need hardly say that he is Commandant of the Armed Constabulary. <i>(Sep 24, 1868,Richmond-Atkinson Papers,2/278</i>) [NOTES: H.A. Atkinson]

>  .. they propose ...taking up defensive posts within our own `frontier', reducing the armed constabulary, for they have `no sympathy for a standing army' ... <i>(Jun 30, 1869,Richmond-Atkinson Papers,2/289</i>) [NOTES: J.C. Richmond]

>  <b>BARBER</b> I would say that the Armed Constabulary is now composed of healthy, sober and intelligent men, who are daily improving in discipline and efficiency. <i>(1984,Red Coat to Jungle Green</i>) [NOTES: original citation from 1871 Commandant Moule in AJHR G-5]

>  The lower barrack [at Wellington] is well kept, being occupied by the Armed Constabulary, but the upper barracks are falling rapidly into decay. <i>(Dec 22, 1986,Dominion,7</i>) [NOTES: Original quote from 1871 William Fielding Diary]

>  <b>BULLER</b> There are ... a chain of [12] military posts [between Tauranga and Napier], all of which are manned by detachments of the `armed mounted constabulary'. <i>(1878,Forty Years in NZ,11</i>)

>  <b>COWAN</b> He became captain in 1863, and served in the Military Settlers, and later in the Armed Constabulary as Sub-Inspector. <i>(1922,NZ Wars, The,1/177</i>)

>  <b>PINFOLD</b> ... so they [Te Whiti followers] were arrested by the armed constabulary and imprisoned. <i>(1932,Fifty Years In Maoriland,23</i>)

>  <b>LAMBERT</b> Major Goring, who was then in charge of the Armed Constabulary at Onepoto, ... determined to ... explore these unknown regions. <i>(1936,Pioneering Reminiscences of Old Wairoa,41</i>)

>  <b>NORRIS</b> With the transfer of the Armed Constabulary headquarters from Hamilton, this building, with much else of the A.C. buildings, was removed to Cambridge. <i>(1964,Settlers in Depression,27</i>)

>  SUTHERLAND, Donald (1839-1919). Backwoodsman. ... Like so many more of his kind he became embroiled in the Maori Wars, [332] and when fighting was finished he left the Armed Constabulary and took a job ... on a Government steamer. <i>(1966,Encyclopaedia of New Zealand, An,3/331</i>)

>  The redoubt [at Taupo]was constructed by the Armed Constabulary in about 1870. <i>(Oct 26, 1972,Dominion,13/4</i>)

>  <b>BELICH</b> In 1867, this miscellany of units was greatly reduced in numbers and formed into a colonial regular army, euphemistically named `the Armed Constabulary'. <i>(1986,NZ Wars, The,214</i>)

>  <b>SORRENSON</b> Peter's father had gone to Urenui as part of the Armed Constabulary; even though he had taken his discharge, he could still be called into service against the Maoris in the event of an emergency. <i>(1986,Na To Hoa Aroha,1/13</i>)

>  <b>MEREDITH</b> Things looked a trifle ugly [at Wanganui over Maori land rights] for a short time; the armed constabulary were mustered and dispatched to the vicinity to be in readiness for any emergency . <i>(1875,Adventuring in Maoriland in the Seventies,83</i>) [NOTES: 1935 ed.]

>  Te Kooti was pursued by Armed Constabulary and Ngati Porou forces ...until he escaped to the King Country in May 1872. <i>(1983,Land of the Mist</i>)



 
 <i>n.</i>

>  [Caption] A number of former Armed Constabulary huts also adorn the [Taupo] hotel ground. <i>(Oct 26, 1972,Dominion</i>)

>  [Caption] The Armed Constabulary Redoubt can be seen at left across the river. <i>(Jun 2002,NZ Memories,57</i>)

>  In October 1867 'The Armed Constabulary Act' was introduced and this enabled Governor Grey to recruit men to serve as an armed constabulary force for the purpose of: "<i>Putting down rebellion, quelling disturbances, preserving the peace, preventing robberies and other felonies, and apprehending offenders against the peace.</i>" <i>(Jun 2002,NZ Memories,58</i>)

>  ...the expensive Armed Constabulary (AC) field force was redeployed piecemeal to occupy a line of posts and stations from Poverty Bay and Hawke's Bay to Lake Taupo and across to the Bay of Plenty. <i>(Oct 4, 2003,Listener (New Zealand Listener),29</i>)

## aroha
 
 <i>n.</i> love, sympathy 1. n. Love, yearning for and absent relative or friend. ... 2. Pity, compassion. ... 3. Affectionate regard. ... 4. v.i. Feel love or pity. 5. v.t. Show approval.] [OED2 1978 cit only at aloha: aloha 1825.] Sympathy, understanding or love

>  If they had not been warmed by the aroha, the multitude outside would have got pneumonia. <i>(Feb 18, 1995,Listener (New Zealand Listener),56</i>)

>  We're not asking for aroha. We're saying: "Here's the deal." <i>(Dec 1996,Mana,No. 11 : 82</i>)

>  The two companies combined in a creative curtain call to send out a message of aroha. <i>(Jun 14, 2001,Dominion,25</i>)

>  His Worship the Mayor Sir Barry Curtis, Deputy Mayor Anne Candy, Manukau City Coucillors, City Manager Colin Dale, Director of City Services Wayne Goodley and council staff, extend all their aroha and awhinatia to John's whanau, especially his hoa wahine Raiha, tamariki and mokopuna. <i>(Dec 6, 2005,NZ Herald,C8</i>)

>  E-huf-fe[acute], Love. <i>(1804,English Colony in NSW,559</i>) [NOTES: NZ Vocabulary]

>  <b>NICHOLAS</b> Affection, love Aro[acute]ha <i>(1817,New Zealand (various authors/titles),2/329</i>) [NOTES: English-NZ Vocabulary]

>  <b>LEE & KENDALL</b> Ar|oha, s. ... Love. <i>(1820,Grammar & Vocabulary of the Language of NZ,136</i>)

>  <b>BOULTBEE</b> fond - eroa' [aroha] <i>(1835,Journal,109</i>)

>  <b>WILLIAMS</b> Saw Aroha the young woman who has been ill for a considerable time, ... but we could not communicate with her; ... <i>(Dec 14, 1832,Early Journals of Henry Williams, The,267</i>)

>  <b>WILY & MAUNSELL</b> `Now, dearest George, adieu. All send much aroha. [fn] Love. <i>(Mar 28, 1863,Robert Maunsell,150</i>) [NOTES: Mrs Maunsell letter in Robert Maunsell (1938)]

>  <b>BAUCKE</b> Sing songs of sentiment, with deft allusion to that tender yearning of the heart, that mystery, which answers to the call of `Aroha' (love). <i>(1905,Where the White Man Treads,90</i>)

>  <b>MASON</b> AROHA: Queenie: the young people are just off. Go and give your aroha to Miss Sylvia. <i>(1963,Pohutukawa Tree,48</i>)

>  aroha ... n. love [Maori]. <i>(1968,NZ Contemp. Dict. Suppl. (Collins),2</i>)

>  <b>BAXTER</b> And the rainbow of aroha shine on each one's face/ Because love is in the look, ... <i>(1969,Collected Poems,442</i>) [NOTES: 1969 in 1979 Baxter Collected Poems]

>  <b>SHADBOLT</b> His [Baxter's] journey into aroha took him first among the lame dogs and junkies sheltering in the decadent housing of central Auckland city ... <i>(1976,Love and Legend,174</i>)

>  aroha <i>(Feb 1981,Avondale College Slang Words in Use</i>) [NOTES: (Auckland) (Goldie Brown Collection)]

>  <b>BAXTER</b> aroha love <i>(1982,Selected Poems,188</i>) [NOTES: Glossary]

>  aroha n. N.Z. love, compassion, or affectionate regard. [Maori] <i>(1982,Collins Concise English Dictionary,56</i>) [NOTES: 1986 NZPOD 35; 1985 Collins NZ Compact Dict. 26]

>  <b>HULME</b> 178 Aroha = love <i>(1983,Bone People, The,467</i>) [NOTES: Translation of Maori Words]

>  <b>HOWE</b> Aroha - `love' is an inadequate translation. Aroha refers to love for one's own family group, but the tendency now is to use it to refer to one's love for all people, i.e, goodwill. It combines the positive qualities of respect, kindness, hospitality, help and the desire to do good for other, without thought or desire for reciprocal action. It is charity of feelings. (I have yet to hear it used in a sexual sense). Aroha comes from knowing that one's kin shares common ancestors <i>(1985,Towards Taha Maori,19</i>) [NOTES: Towards `Taha Maori' in English (NZATE)]

>  Crime, violence and aroha in Shannon. <i>(Dec 6, 1986,Listener (New Zealand Listener),17</i>)

>  <b>HULME</b> Five weeks, I'd thought then, just five weeks and already the aroha grows. <i>(1986,Te Kaihau,94</i>)

>  Polynesian peals of laughter ... and aroha permeating everything. <i>(1987,Metro,6</i>) [NOTES: Keogh, ChiaroscuroMetro Fiction 6]

>  <b>BURCHFIELD</b> aroha /|aroha/. N.Z. [Maori, cogn. w. Hawaiian aloha.] Affection, love; sympathy. In Hawaii the phoneme /l/ was sometimes perceived in the late 18c. and the early 19c. as /r/. Hawaiian examples of aloha are not given below. <i>(1988,Lexicographical & Linguistic Studies,191</i>)

>  <b>BURCHFIELD</b> aroha /|aroha/. N.Z. [Maori, cogn. w. Hawaiian aloha.] Affection, love; sympathy. In Hawaii the phoneme /l/ was sometimes perceived in the late 18c. and the early 19c. as /r/. Hawaiian examples of aloha are not given below. <i>(1988,Lexicographical & Linguistic Studies,191</i>)

>  <b>ELLIS</b> The usual salutation is Aroha (attachment). <i>(1826,Narrative of Tour through Hawaii,xii. 357</i>)

>  <b>DRUMMOND</b> Mary Anne Parker .. emerges from this journal - and from her own unselfconscious account of life in early Auckland - as a remarkable woman in many ways, with an aroha for the Maori people that is remembered still. <i>(1971,Auckland Journals,265</i>) [NOTES: Vicesimus Lush]

>  <b>IHIMAERA</b> They will look at you with love, and aroha, for you have honoured them. <i> Ibid.</i> iv. 16 There still remain a number who cherish the ideals of family living and family aroha. <i>(1974,Whanau,II.8</i>)

>  Roy and children and Gardiner family gratefully thank all relatives, neighbours and friends for your personal attendance, floral tributes, cards, telegrams and donations of aroha in our recent sad loss of much-loved wife, mother, daughter and sister. <i>(Jan 8, 1977,Whanau,4-12/8</i>)

>  Please offer my warmest aroha to Miss Gilhooly, and thank her for looking after you sowell. <i>(Aug 1978,Islands,13</i>)

>  <b>BAUCKE</b> Neither can you define mercy, pity, affection else than by the verb `aroha' (love). <i>(1905,Where the White Man Treads,23</i>)

## Art Union
 
 <i>n.</i> the name for the former NZ state lottery.

>  <i>(Dec 26, 1931,Dominion,13/7</i>) [NOTES: [OED2]]

>  <b>FINLAYSON</b> The poor little settlement ... hadn't seen ... such lavishness since Timi Kaituna won the Art Union. <i>(1937,Brown Man's Burden,29,1973 ed.</i>) [NOTES: `Wi Gets the Gospel']

>  Instruments scrounged, donated, bought through profits of [prisoner-of-war] camp raffles and art unions. <i>(1945,Gunner Inglorious,108</i>)

>  <b>FAIRBURN</b> I'm told it,s mainly art union money, ... <i>(Nov 26, 1947,Letters,176,1981 ed.</i>)

>  USE OF TERM `ART UNION' The use of the term art union was under discussion at a meeting of the Auckland Society of the Arts ... Some time ago a letter was sent to the Minister of Internal Affairs asking if the term could be restricted in its use to that originally intended, namely the disposal of works of fine art. In his reply the Minister said: `The term is well established and its meaning well known in the community, and therefore it is not thought necessary to make specific change at this juncture.' Commenting on the present use of the term, the president ... said that it was obviously incorrect and that the present `art unions' were really lotteries. <i>(Oct 28, 1947,Evening Post,8/1</i>)

>  How did the art union, now replaced by the Government's `Golden Kiwi' lottery, come by its name? Officers of the Internal Affairs Department said today they believed that under the law operating when the art unions began in 1929, raffles or lotteries could only have works of art for prizes. Alluvial gold had apparently qualified as a work of art and for some years the prizes were [pounds]4000 in alluvial gold, which had to be deposited in the Bank of New Zealand and paid for before any tickets could be sold. Cash finally replaced the alluvial gold prizes, but the name `art union' stuck. <i>(Dec 1, 1961,Evening Post,17</i>)

>  <b>STONE</b> The same kind of double morality can be observed in the conflicting attitudes towards lotteries. ... They have ... set their seal of approval on what is euphemistically known as an `art union'. <i>(1959,Verdict on NZ,14</i>)

>  <b>CASEY</b> I won fifty pounds in an art union once ... <i>(1963,As Short a Spring,279</i>)

>  <b>SARGESON</b> Art Union: lottery. <i>(1964,Collected Stories,297</i>) [NOTES: Pearson Glossary]

>  <b>McLINTOCK</b> The term `art union' is today used in a wider sense than the sense in which it is employed in the gaming legislation. The art union proper as defined in the Gaming Act exists only to a limited extent in New Zealand, and is confined to a few academies or societies of fine arts. What has generally become known as the art union is the national lottery. The term `art union' is due to the fact that originally the prizes in the lottery were alluvial gold. These major art unions are now known as the Golden Kiwi lottery. .. In 1961 the Government decided to replace the art unions with the present Golden Kiwi lottery. <i>(1966,Encyclopaedia of New Zealand, An,3/347</i>)

>  <b>TURNER</b> The term art union is shared with Australia; few remember the times when the prize was a painting. <i>(1966,English Language in Aust. & NZ, The,175</i>)

>  <b>FINLAYSON</b> art union: lottery <i>(1973,Brown Man's Burden,140</i>) [NOTES: Pearson's Glossary]

>  <i>(Jun 6, 1981,Listener (New Zealand Listener),112</i>) [NOTES: [I.A. Gordon has a long article on derivation and history.]]

>  art union n. Austral. & N.Z. a lottery for prizes usually of goods (in Australia) or cash (in New Zealand). <i>(1982,Collins Concise English Dictionary,58</i>) [NOTES: 1986 NZPOD 37; 1985 Collins NZ Compact Dict. 26]

>  Art Union (forerunner of Golden Kiwi Lottery) <i>Ibid.</i> 6 Once when she couldn't have me Dad let me go to the flicks by myself. He gave me some extra money, so I went to the milkbar and bought a gobstopper and some other lollies. I stuffed my face, and felt crook all the way home. He bought new footy boots for my eldest brother too, and an Art Union ticket for Mum. He must have had a beaut time up the RSA that day, I reckon. <i>(1988,University Entrance Board Bursaries Exam,English 3</i>)

>  <b>JACKSON</b> When he failed ... to win Tatts or the Art Union, he took it as a personal slight ... <i>(1988,Rainshadow,105</i>)

>  <b>WATSON</b> I bought art union tickets every pay-day. <i>(1965,Stand in the Rain,111</i>)



 
 <i>n.</i> = Jimmy Allen's Art Union, q.v.

>  [Seeing so many here in France in easy jobs] makes a man ... wish that he had stayed in New Zealand until drawn in the Art Union. <i>(Dec 16, 1917,Great Adventure,275</i>) [NOTES: Carruthers]



 
<b>2</b> <i>n.</i> a lottery with alluvial gold as a prize; then mainly used as the euphemism for the NZ state lottery from about 1929 to 195? [[W3: Any of several 19c. American associations for he distribution of paintings and prints by lottery; nf OED, COD; nf P8.] [AND lottery 1849, 1872 (paintings 1841); OED2 art 18. a union of persons for the purpose of promoting art by purchasing the works of artists, and distributing them among their members, which is usu. done by lottery; in Australia and New Zealand, a lottery with cash prizes 1839 NZ 1931.]

>  <i>(Aug 2, 1866,Lyttelton Times,2/4</i>) [NOTES: fide A.S. Helm: to be checked]

>  `By permission of the Colonial Secretary Miss Bacon [of the School of Art Needlework, Auckland] will hold a GRAND ART UNION OF WORKS OF ART, comprising handpainted brackets, diapers, cushions, wax work, art needlework. Tickets 2s 6d.' <i>(1886,Napier Daily Telegraph</i>) [NOTES: quoted in 1974 Wood Victorian New Zealanders 18]



 
<b>2</b> <i>n.</i> a local raffle or lottery, often for a charitable cause

>  [Advt] Art Union of Sewing Machines.Second Distribution. Fourteen First-ClassSewing Machines as Prizes. Value - OneHundred Pounds. Tickets - 10s. each. <i>(Sep 4, 1874,NZ Herald,1</i>)

>  <b>BLACKLEY</b> There have been 'art unions' of Racingsweeps - of fancy soaps, of books, offurniture & o ye gods & little fishes, ofcakes and confectionery here. <i>(1992,Art of Alfred Sharpe, The,26</i>) [NOTES: From 1888 SHARPE in Newcastle Morning Herald]

>  <b>FRAME</b> The Art Union? There was a theory that if you bought a ticket up north where the population was thickest you were sure to win a prize. The raffle? Tatts? <i>(1960,Owls Do Cry,73</i>)

>  Sweeps on horse-races and so-called art unions were increasing to an alarming extent, and in Westland more than in any other part of the colony. <i>(1877,NZ Parliamentary Debates,XXV/245</i>)

>  A gentleman in Christchurch, who had five tickets in the Dunedin art union in connection with the Art Gallery, received an intimation that he was the lucky drawer of five prizes... This is an experience unexampled in art union drawings anywhere in the world. <i>(Dec 11, 1890,Otago Witness,23</i>)

>  For cool impudence, a book agent on the warpath with a valuable work in 55 parts is hard to beat, but if there is anybody who can "go one better" it is the art union lottery man with 20,000 tickets to sell at a shilling a piece. The book agent does his own bullying, and wheedling; the art unionist asks you to do it for him on a 20 percent commission. . . Accompanying this letter. . is a printed circular setting forth the merits of the engravings. "It is certain," says the lottery man, "that many a mind will warm with enthusiasm on becoming possessed of such admirable works of art, which are now placed within the reach of a discerning public at the trifling risk of one shilling." <i>(Sep 25, 1890,Otago Witness,23</i>)

>  Wellington's best known seller of art union tickets declares that her job is still a most interesting one. <i>(Oct 16, 1935,NZ Free Lance,20</i>)

>  The subscribers to the Ceramic and Crystal Palace Art Union will be glad to learn.. that their selections and prizes have been shipped in the Isle of Bute. <i>(Apr 19, 1879,Auckland Weekly News,8</i>)

>  Two Parnell ladies.. have dropped each other's acquaintance over a quarrel about a ten-shilling art union prize. <i>(Jan 2, 1904,NZ Observer,7</i>)

>  The secretary of a hospital bazaar committee was fined for submitting a prize cow as a fit prize for an "art union." <i>(Aug 9, 1913,NZ Observer,7</i>)

>  Mr Smith.. is not charging anything for his open sesame to the winning of prizes in art unions. <i>(Dec 24, 1924,Christchurch Star,14</i>)

>  Our raffles, or miscalled Art Unions are doomed. <i>(Jul 7, 1926,NZ Observer,6</i>)

>  [Advt.] Positively the Last Art Union with a GBP5000 Prize List. <i>(Nov 27, 1926,NZ Dairy Produce Exporter,60</i>)

>  [Advt.] Grand GBP5,000 Alluvial Gold Art Union. <i>(Mar 13, 1935,NZ Free Lance,50b</i>)

>  I understand that the Government is absolutely against State lotteries and yet I have just taken a ticket in what is described as an 'art union' - and where the 'art' comes from I do not know...The visitor was informed that the application of "art union" was a survival from the old practice under which works of art were raffled. <i>(Jul 17, 1939,Te Aroha Times,3</i>)

>  <b>WOLFE</b> Gambling was officially sanctified in 1961 when the old Art Union was renamed the Golden Kiwi. <i>(1991,Kiwi,59</i>)

>  The origin of the affair [ = a practical joke] was, we understand, in connection with the Art Union of last week. <i>(Jan 4, 1864,Marlborough Express,6</i>)

>  [Correspondence] A LOTTERY `SWINDLE'. ... The `Art Union' system in confectionary [sic] is understood by all reasonable people to be one of risk as the whole or perhaps a large portion of that manufactured may never be required, if tickets are not sold - and therefore a considerble margin of risk of the kind is always allowed. Our profit, if any, has yet to be ascertained. Next week if we get in the price of our tickets we will be able to tell. [The raffle or Art Union was apparently for gingerbread or gingerbread cake as a prize.] <i>(Jan 4, 1868,Marlborough Express,5</i>)

>  THE ART UNION SYSTEM NOT LEGAL. - The Auckland Weekly News says:- `We have been requested to direct the attention of subscribers to Mr. Lewisson's Gift Enterprise, which was to have been drawn today ... The moneys paid by subscribers will be refunded ... <i>(Jan 11, 1868,Marlborough Express,3</i>)

>  [advt] Trentham Dominion Scholarships Art Union ... First Prize Value [pounds]1000 Tickets 1/- each. <i>(Jun 10, 1919,Quick March,89</i>)

>  ... An art union with attractive prizes has been arranged. <i>(Mar 10, 1921,Quick March,39</i>)

>  [Advertisement for an Art Union to beautify the Cambridge domain] Kia Ora [pounds]5000 Art Union./ Dig down for that half-crown right now. Be in on the last [pounds]5000 Art Union without fail. <i>(Sep 15, 1926,Aussie (NZ Section),XV</i>)

## artist
 
 <i>n.</i> An expert (informal): `Jack was an artist with a bulldozer'. Usage: `he was a well-known booze artist' (=heavy drinker) usu. with qualifying epithet, indicating an expert in or devotee of the field named, often with derogatory connotations suggesting illegality, impropriety, excess; or with ironic connotations suggesting a hard-case or inveterate performer in a field disapproved of, aself-styled expert. (Cf. earlier -fiend, -king, merchant.) [esp.WW1 one-pip artist, one-star artist, second lieutenant. [nf W3, WF] [AND 1889 Orig. US: see OED2 10. Austral., N.Z., and U.S. 1890 (US).]]

>  <b>TREADWELL</b> Second lieutenants, or, as these most important gentlemen became known during the war, `one-pip artists' ...... many [Victoria College OTC volunteers] were soon after given the opportunity of joining some other company in the Wellington Infantry Battalion as second lieutenants, or, as these most important gentlemen became known during the war, `one-pip artists'. <i>(1936,Recollections of an Amateur Soldier,3</i>) [NOTES: 1914-18]

>  [quoting a WW1 publication] Scene: Any dugout. ... `as a matter of fact I used to be a bit of a blackboard artist'. [a pun may be indicated, but the use is not meant to be literal] <i>(May 25, 1942,2NZEF Times,4</i>)

>  [Slang of the N.Z.E.F.] There is a touch of irreverence in `the trump of the dump', for O.C., and in `one-star artist'; ... <i>(Apr 2, 1938,Press, The,18</i>) [NOTES: McNab NZ Slang]

>  <b>FULLARTON</b> Hood; you bloody clifty artist, where are my matches? <i>(1944,Troop Target,25</i>)

>  <b>HENDERSON</b> Shortie, the one-pip artist, comes up to us around the gun. <i>(1945,Gunner Inglorious,24,1974 ed.</i>)

>  <b>SYDER& HODGETTS</b> A man who drinks beer in large quantities as part of a regular routine. .in 4;`He's a good bloke gone to seed, he can't keep off the beer there days, just a booze-artist.' <i>(1962,Australian & NZ English [TS],92,1974 ed.</i>)

>  <b>McGILL</b> one star artist second lieutenant, WWI. <i>(1988,Dictionary of Kiwi Slang, A,79</i>)

>  In the last few months - since Percy took up the T.P. jewellery business - he had come to be regarded as something of a `hot air artist and customarily made the statement that he carried five hundred quids' worth of jewellery in his bag. <i>(Sep 14, 1912,NZ Truth,377/6/6</i>)

>  <b>REID</b> Then we heard the wireless news,/ Old bluff-artist Winston giving his views. <i>(1964,Book of New Zealand, A (J.C. Reid),287</i>) [NOTES: [1941] Reid Book NZ 1964 (The Tale of Doom)]

>  <b>DAVIN</b> A real artist for the booze, isn't he? <i>(1949,Roads from Home,49</i>)

>  <b>McGILL</b> <i>bash artist</i> brutal user of fists on other; eg `Watch out for Morton after he's had a few drinks, becomes a real bash artist, clock you as soon as look at you.' <i>(1988,Dictionary of Kiwi Slang, A</i>)

 <i>n.</i> artillery

>  <b>SLATTER</b> ... there were more rivers to cross and more men to be maimed up there where the arty was throbbing the sky. <i>(1959,Gun in My Hand, A,92</i>)



## aruhe
 
 <i>n.</i> <i>Aruhe ... Edible fern root</i>, being the root of <i>Pteridium aquilinum</i> var. <i>esculentum</i> [-] (See [fernroot](../F#fernroot))

>  <b>PARKINSON</b> Heanoohe ?mis-spg or misprint for Hearoohe) A Vocabulary of the Language of New Zealand. [...] Egooree, <i>A dog</i>. Teyka, <i>Fish</i>. Hewhai[a-grave], <i>A skate</i>. Eraperape, <i>A fish called Chimaera</i>. Hepaooa, <i>Ear-shells</i>. Hekohooa[a-grave], <i>Small ear-shells</i>. [...] Heanoohe[e1-grave], <i>Fern root.</i> [...] Hewai, <i>Water</i>. [...] Tochee, <i>A hatchet, or adze</i>. Hecacahoo, <i>A garment</i>. Opoonamoo, <i>A green ear-ring</i>. Potai, <i>A feather ornament on their head</i>. Emaho, Tataow. Hewaca, <i>A canoe.</i> [...] Orero, <i>To speak, or a speech</i>. [...] Ekeeno[e2-grave], <i>Bad.</i> <i>(1770,Journal,126</i>)

>  <b>NICHOLAS</b> Fern-root Aro[acute]hhe <i>(1817,New Zealand (various authors/titles),2/329</i>)

>  <b>LEE & KENDALL</b> [|prec.= acute, '= stress] Ad|ue, s. Fern-root. <i>(1820,Grammar & Vocabulary of the Language of NZ,132</i>)

>  <b>DIEFFENBACH</b> Aruhe, <i>also</i> aroi, arohi, <i>and</i> arue - fernroot <i>(1843,Travels in NZ,357</i>) [NOTES: Dictionary NZ Language]

>  <b>HOOKER</b> Aruhe <i>Col[enso]. Pteris aquilina. </i> <i>(1867,Handbook for New Zealand, The,764</i>) [NOTES: List of Names]

>  Aruhe. Pteris aquilina, Linn. var. esculenta <i>(1869,TrNZI,1/III/52</i>) [NOTES: Buchanan Botany Otago]

>  The Korari is the name of the flower stalk, often, but wrongly, used as the name of the plant. The edible root is the Aruhe. The word `Muka' is commonly applied to the dressed fibre; it is the correct name for the inner leaves. Cleared bush-land is suitable for the cultivation of the flax plant, and even swamp-land is improved by drainage. In the various flax swamps near nelson ...the finest plants grow upon the soil that has been thrown out of the ditch <i>(1870,TrNZI,2/117</i>) [NOTES: Heaphy NZ Flax]

>  <b>BEATTIE</b> Aruhe - the edible fernroot. <i>(1945,Maori Place-Names of Canterbury,64</i>) [NOTES: c1880 South Island Maori Lists `Wahi Mahika Kai' in 1945 Beattie]

>  [Gleichenia] is soon overpowered by the [bracken], and the entire area is quickly covered with a luxuriant growth of `aruhe,' thus affording a suggestion as to the way in which the wide fern-clad `pakihis' were originally formed <i>(1895,TrNZI,28/15</i>)

>  <b>CHEESEMAN</b> <i>[Pteris aquilina. Linn</i>. var. <i>esculenta</i>, Hook.f.] Common fern; Bracken; Rau-aruhe; Rahurahu; of the root, <i>Aruhe, Roi. </i> <i>(1906,Manual NZ Flora,971</i>)

>  They could [22] have satisfied most of the `kinaki' [ed fn: Relish eaten with other food] wants from the coast, but preferred forest products. You found the incoming Ngati-Porou along the coast following the fishing grounds and the warm areas for kumara and taro cultivation, while the `tangata whenua' stuck to the hinter-land and forests, faring on `mamaku' [ed fn: Edible tree fern (<i>Cyathea medullaris</i>)], `aruhe', eels and huahua. <i>(May 22, 1930,Na To Hoa Aroha,2/21,1987 ed.</i>) [NOTES: Ngata-Buck Correspondence (Ngata)]

>  <i>aruhe</i>, a plant (Pteridium esculentum), edible fernroot; bracken-fern. <i>(1946,Journal of the Polynesian Society,55/151</i>) [NOTES: Andersen Maori Words in English]

>  <b>RICHARDS</b> <i>PTERIDIUM ... aquilinum</i> ... var. <i>esculenta</i> ... Bracken. ARUHE, RAUARUHE. <i>(1952,Chatham Islands,14</i>)

>  aruke[sic] n -s [Maori aruhe] New Zeal : the starchy rhizome of the brake (Pteridium aquilinum) <i>(1961,Merriam-Webster Third International,125</i>)

>  <b>BURTON</b> [Title] Aruhe (fernroot) <i>(1982,Two Hundred Years of NZ Food & Cookery,5</i>)

>  <b>FULLER</b> Perei and maikaika, types of orchids, were sought after. The perei had a mass of tubers which were cooked in the same way as aruhe. <i>(1978,Maori Food and Cookery,7 ed.</i>)



## arum
 
 <i>n.</i> The Pacific taro (<i>Colocasia</i> spp.), is an aroid, a member of the fam. Araceae, of which the <i>Arum</i> genus is the type. [Rare.]

>  The <i>Arum</i> is planted in little circular concaves, ... [ed. fn: the <i>taro</i>.] <i>(Oct 21, 1769,Cook's Journals,1/583,1955 ed.</i>) [NOTES: W.B. Monkhouse in 1955 Cook Journal]



## arum lily
 
 <i>n.</i> Zantedeschia aethiopica, arum, arum lily, and occas. white arum, are the preferred names in NZ for the introduced plant which is rarely here, and often elsewhere, called (OED `erroneously') lily(earlier <i>ethiopian</i> or<i> trumpet lily</i>), which often grows so profusely as to be regarded as a weed. Standard Common Names for Weeds (p.2) refers white arum to this entry: and distinguishes (p.2) the rarer introduced <i>Italian arum, Arum italicum</i> Mill.

>  West went round the place and planted Kopuko-puko [ed note: Kopuka-puka Chat. I. lilies or giant forgetmenot, Myosotideum nobile], arums and sweet peas in the afternoon. <i>(Nov 1, 1882,Chudleigh Diary,313</i>)

>  <b>HYDE</b> Through the cool glades of its grounds you catch glimpses of arum lilies, ... <i>(1934,Journalese,16</i>)

>  <b>MULGAN</b> Arum lilies grew rankly in the open in many a garden, or bared their purity beside rusting rubbish. <i>(1934,Spur of Morning,43</i>)

>  <i>arum [=] arum lily [s] </i>... <i>arum lily [s] Zantedeschia aethiopica (L.) </i> Spreng. <i>(1969,Standard Common Names for Weeds,2</i>)



## arvo
 
 <i>n.</i> (informal) afternoon [ORIGIN: abbreviation, 1953 17 M A35 Thames DHS 15 SARVO (THE SARVO);(1962-63); Syder and Hodgetts Aust and NZ English [TS]; 1974 1031 -->This afternoon. .in 4;`See you this arvo. About three o'clock, O.K.?']

>  arvo, this arvo ... (Sl. phr.) this afternoon. <i>(1968,NZ Contemp. Dict. Suppl. (Collins),2</i>)

>  arvo, this arvo ... (sl. phr.) this afternoon. <i>(1966,NZ Contemp. Dict. (Aust.-NZ Suppl.),610</i>)

## as
 
 <i>adv.</i> an intensifier in the construction '[adjective] + as' (elliptical for the full form of comparison, 'as [adjective] as [NP]'); initially in the phrase sweet as (q.v.), extended to other adjectives as illustrated below [with adverbs also (slowly as)?]

>  "I was close as to Wallace" he says. <i>(Mar 10, 2002,Sunday Star Times,C1</i>)

>  Putting down the phone after an irate caller: "Rude as." <i>(Oct 17, 2001,TV2</i>)

>  <b>Ihimaera</b> Strong as an ox. Stubborn as. Fast with his fists. <i>(2000,Uncle's Story, The,138</i>)

>  National's campaign pamphlet featuring English posed around a barbecue with his wife was "cheesy as", leading Bidois to conclude the party would stoop to anything to "scab a vote." <i>(Jul 20, 2002,Weekend Herald,B9</i>)

>  <b>WELCH</b> "Tricky as," he added, thus locking onto his first New Zealandism. <i>(1999,Human Remains,9</i>)

>  <b>McGILL</b> as : The conjunction minus the expected comparative clause, used to maximise or increase the impact of a statement; as in <i>I was hungry as</i>. <i>(1998,David McGill's Complete Kiwi Slang Dictionary,9</i>)

## ashet
 
 <i>n.</i> A serving dish or platter; a meat-dish, a large meat-serving plate, large plate or dish for serving (roast) meat on. [Otago-Southland.]

>  <b>DRUMMOND</b> <i>(1967,At Home In NZ</i>) [NOTES: [quoting an early diarist: not apparently Jane Bannerman](Griffiths collection)]

>  <b>HENDERSON</b> On a mahogany counter on blue willow-patterned ashets lay the odd shapes and sizes ... <i>(1982,Open Country,155</i>) [NOTES: (Percy Hancock story `A Penny to Spend') 155 (Griffiths collection)]

>  <b>BATHGATE</b> `Surely the laddie'll ha'e his supper first, Mr. Tamson,' said Mirren, as she put on the table an ashet containing savoury-smelling mutton pies. <i>(1913,Sodger Sandy's Bairn,12</i>)

>  <b>HISLOP</b> [Stanley's Hotel at Macraes Flat, Central Otago] was one of the best tucker houses on the road, and with bacon and eggs for breakfast, the hungry C.T. who was usually found stopping there would have a full ashet to help himself from, placed before him. <i>(1944,Pure Gold,16</i>)

>  The process of yielding to the environment is illustrated in the case of the word `ashet', meaning a large meat plate. A Dunedin Scottish lady explained : `Mother would have said `ashet' always. I might say `ashet', or I might say `the big meat dish'. The family would say `the big meat dish'. (There are signs, however, that this word is returning to use. <i>(Nov 10, 1950,Listener (New Zealand Listener),19</i>) [NOTES: `W.F.A.' The Scottish Tongue in Otago]

>  <i>(1951,Personal Communication</i>) [NOTES: seen advertised in a Dunedin store]

>  We used ashet for a meat serving dish at home in Palmerston North: my father's parents were from Scotland. <i>(1986,Personal Communication</i>) [NOTES: Jane Godfrey]

>  <b>SUMMERS</b> He looked at the ashet. It was heaped with rice, ... . In the middle ... were deliciously tender lamb shanks ... . <i>(1960,Moon Over the Alps,48</i>) [NOTES: 1974 ed. [also uses byre]

>  ashet n. local NZ meat-dish. [ = dial.] <i>(1986,NZ Pocket Oxford Dictionary,38</i>) [NOTES: nf. 1982 Collins Concise Eng. Dict.]

## aspro
 
 <i>n.</i> ??Obs. Used in various usu. derog. or dismissive collocations. 1. aspro headache, a person tiresome enough to give even an aspro a headache. 2. A dismissive c.p.: hang around and I'll fry you an aspro (aspirin), said to a tiresome person. ca1945 p.c. students and nurses (Wellington) 3. aspro doctor, a lazy or unknowledgeable GP; a quack. ca1935 p.c. Marlborough from the easy prescription for all ills `Take an aspro and go to bed: seven-and-six please.'

## Assessor
 
 <i>n.</i> A ??Maori paid as a government agent to assist with expert advice in settling claims between Maori and Pakeha. [Hist.] (See [Native Assessor](../N#native-assessor))

>  <b>T.S.GRACE</b> On reaching the village of Parawai, the Native Assessor, who lives here, stopped me to say that he could not get on with the trial of Mr. ---'s case to-morrow unless my son Tom stayed to interpret for him. <i>(Nov 28, 1866,Journal,150</i>) [NOTES: 1928 ed. T.S. Grace Journal in Grace Pioneer Missionary (1928) 28 Nov 150]

>  <b>T.S. GRACE</b> They then went furiously to the 4th point - that we were all hirelings and worked for money like the Assessors. One fellow said, `Don't you get your quarter?' I replied, `Certainly! I do not live by stealing.' `Quarter' has, of late years, become a proverbial term; the Government Native Assessors, being paid once a quarter, talk commonly of getting their quarters. The Kingites despise these Maoris who have been bribed over, and hate and deride the `quarter' as conclusive of all that is bad. Hanauru, the King's great priest, who was present, pointed out how he could go about anywhere, all the year round, and wanted no quarter. <i>(Aug 1877,Journal,266</i>) [NOTES: T.S. Grace Journal in Grace Pioneer Missionary (1928) Aug 266]

## assignat
 
 <i>n.</i> Ironic use of assignat paper money issued by the revolutionary government of France, on security of the state lands OED2 1790.

>  <b>HARGREAVES</b> The [Governor Fitzroy's (1844)] Debentures, popularly known as `government rags', were given various nicknames such as assignats, `shinplasters', and `flash notes'. All these terms had reference to worthless paper money, some of which had been issued in the past. Assignats was the name given to large amounts of paper money issued in France during the French Revolution, but which depreciated to such as extent to be almost worthless. `Shinplasters'--sometimes written `skinplasters'--had its origin in North America, and again referred to depreciated paper money, while `flash notes' was a popular term in use for any counterfeit money or worthless forged paper money. <i>(1972,From Beads to Banknotes,40</i>)

## assisted
 
 <i>adj.</i> [[AND assisted emigrant 1848; OED2 assisted 1853.] Of or pertaining to (usu. State) subsidised passages to NZ.]

>  <b>FITTON</b> In the Chapter on `Assisted Emigration' will be found the regulations issued by Mr. Harman, respecting assisted passages for labouring persons and domestic servants to Canterbury [ = in Harman's Information Respecting Canterbury]. <i>Ibid.</i> 295 [Hdg] ASSISTED EMIGRATION TO THE VARIOUS SETTLEMENTS. <i>(1856,New Zealand (various authors/titles),211</i>)

>  <b>MILLER</b> Besides the forecastle where the sailors slept, the sleeping accommodation usually consisted of six cabins twelve feet by twelve with windows overlooking the stern, a house on the deck for the second-class passengers, and several long dormitories crowded with narrow shakedowns for the assisted emigrants in a ship of 250 to 500 tons. <i>(1958,Early Victorian NZ,36</i>)

>  <b>ADAM</b> Assisted emigration will likely take the place of free emigration; ... <i>(1876,25 Years of Emigrant Life,56</i>) [NOTES: 2nd ed.]

>  Wanted assisted emigrants to proceed to the Canterbury Province. <i>(1881,Battle of Life,69</i>)



 
 <i>adj.</i>

>  With more than 500 assisted immigrants..the Government-chartered ship Atlantisberthed at Wellington shortly after 10a.m. yesterday. Included among the maleassisted immigrants were coalminers,timber mill hands,.. footwear operatorsand labourers for essential industries. <i>(Feb 9, 1948,Dominion,6</i>)

>  <b>FITTON</b> [quoting Otago Witness] ASSISTED IMMIGRATION:- <i>(1856,New Zealand (various authors/titles),312</i>)

>  The following table shows the immigration - distinguishing between the unassisted and the assisted - Year. .. Unassisted Immigrants. .. Free and Assisted Immigrants. .. Total Immigrants. <i>(1892,NZ Official Handbook,60</i>)

>  <b>ANDERSON</b> Assisted immigrants of course had to travel steerage, and the food was not the best. <i>(1964,Doctor in the Mountains,27</i>) [NOTES: 1907 (1974) in 1964]

>  They embarked as "government" or assisted immigrants, their passages arranged and subsidised by colonial authorities. <i>(Sep 17, 1990,Listener (New Zealand Listener),20</i>)

>  <b>HALL</b> The 'Captain Cook' - thirteen thousandtons of rust, jammed with eleven hundredassisted migrants, and manned with a crewpress-ganged from the Gorbals. <i>(1980,Prisoner of Mother England,3</i>)



## assisted passage
 
 <i>n.</i>

>  <b>FITTON</b> In the Chapter on `Assisted Emigration' will be found the regulations issued by Mr. Harman, respecting assisted passages for labouring persons and domestic servants to Canterbury [ = in Harman's Information Respecting Canterbury.] <i>Ibid.</i> 296 In addition ... some funds have been sent to England for the payment of Assisted Passages to [Auckland and New Plymouth] as well as to other parts of New Zealand. <i>Ibid.</i> [Canterbury regulations] 1. Assisted passages will be granted by the Provincial Government to bona fide [a-circumflex] labouring men, country mechanics, their wives and families, and female domestic servants only. [1/2 the passage money] <i>(1856,New Zealand (various authors/titles),22</i>)

>  NOTICE TO NEWLY ARRIVED IMMIGRANTS NOTICE IS HEREBY GIVEN, that if any Immigrants who have given Promissory Notes in part payment of assisted passages to this Province, and of which payment will be required a year after their landing, are willing to pay the whole or any part of their engagements before that time, they will be allowed a Discount ... <i>(Jul 4, 1857,Lyttelton Times,6</i>)

>  <b>CARR</b> Assisted passages are also granted to farm labourers by the High Commissioner's Office in London ... <i>(1913,Country Work,7</i>)

>  <b>HARTLEY</b> The hard-working colonist who arrived on an assisted passage of a very different sort ... had scant respect for remittance men, whatever their category. <i>(1987,Swagger on Our Doorstep,31</i>)

## astelia
 
 <i>n.</i> As a common name esp. for <i>Astelia nervosa</i>. Also common astelia, giant astelia. See wharawhara.

>  <b>YATE</b> At the tops, and on some of the higher branches of some of the forest-trees, grows a sedgy, succulent plant (<i>Astilia angustifolia</i>), much valued for the sweetness of the stem ...] <i>(1835,New Zealand (various authors/titles),16</i>)

>  The slopes of the hills are usually covered with a dense forest ... the forms being ... Toro (<i>Persoonia Toro</i>, A Cunn.) ... and immense tussocks of an undescribed Astelia, and cutting grasses (<i>Gahnia lacera</i>, Stend., <i>G. arenaria</i>, Hook. f.) ... and Mange-mange (<i>Lygodium articulatum</i>, Swartz.), ... render all progress tedious ... <i>(1870,TrNZI,2/90</i>) [NOTES: Kirk]

>  <b>LAING AND BLACKWELL</b> The <i>Astelias</i>, like many other epiphytic plants, provide against a long drought, by storing up water in the thick, curved bases of their circle of leaves. <i>(1907,Plants of NZ,100</i>) [NOTES: 1940 ed. p. 102]

>  <b>COWAN</b> Some of the Maoris climbed the tree, and from the cover of the thick flax-like growth of wharawhara, or astelia, in the forks of the main branches, fired over the log wall. <i>(1922,NZ Wars, The,I/28</i>)

>  He loved to scramble through the maze of angled rock in a mountain gorge ...that unrestrained world of rock and snow grass and astelia and mountain flax, the shingle screes and the outcrop spurs. <i>(1968,Pagan Game, The,120</i>)

>  <b>DENNIS</b> On damper parts of the forest floor astelias <i>A. solandri</i> and <i>A. grandis</i> [160] are plentiful among the more open, stunted trees. <i>(1981,Paparoas Guide,159</i>)

## aster
 
 <i>n.</i> Var. plants or shrubs with flowers resembling the aster

>  <b>FORSTER</b> ... here and there this slope [in Cascade Cove] was variegated by large branches of a white fine new Aster.[ed. fn] <i>Celmisia holosericea</i> Hook f. <i>(Apr 11, 1773,Resolution Journal of J.R. Forster, The,2/253</i>)

>  <b>GREEN</b> Large white asters (<i>Celmesia</i>)[sic], with flowers four inches in diameter, peeped forth from the boulders [in the Southern Alps]. <i>(1883,High Alps of NZ,173</i>)

>  <b>TAYLOR</b> <i>Celmisia halosericea</i>, Aster <i>(1885,Te Ika a Maui</i>)

>  <b>LINDSAY</b> The extreme toughness of the leaf and the presence of this cottony material [on the under surface] have given rise to the popular [53] colonial name applied to the more familiar species [of <i>Celmisia</i>], - the `Leather' or `Cotton' plant. They are also known partly as the `Native Aster,' - a term applied, however, equally to species of <i>Olearia</i> and <i>Mesembryanthemum.</i> <i>(1868,Contributions to NZ Botany,52</i>)

>  <b>HAAST</b> <i>Celmisia coriacea </i>, and <i>spectabilis</i>, the gigantic New Zealand asters, were also very frequent .. <i>(1879,Geology of the Provinces of Canterbury & Westland,73</i>)

## ataata
 
 <i>n.</i> <i>Lunella smaragda</i>, a large univalve mollusc.] A shellfish. See cat's eye [Ma.].

>  <b>POWELL</b> CAT'S EYE (<i>Lunella smaragda</i>). Best known of all our shells of the intertidal rocks. ... The circular greenish operculum, the cat's eye, is its most conspicuous feature. The Maori name is Ataata. <i>(1947,Native Animals of NZ,26</i>)

## atua
 
 <i>n.</i> 1. n. God, demon, supernatural being, ghost. ... 2. Object of superstitious regard. ... 3. Anything malign, disagreeable.] [The example given is: Toku atua he pakeha. A European is the most disagreeable thing to me.] [Not recorded from Maori on Cook's Voyages: OED2 A Polynesian name for a supernatural being, god or demon.: [ORIGIN: Maori, 1769 June 28 Cook Journals (1955) I/111 fn 5 [?Tahiti] A [dmiralty MS] ... Tupia informs [us] that this is a representation of one of the Second rank of Eatuas or gods called Mauwe ... [Eatua = E atua `It is a/the god'].] A spirit, demon or god [Ma.].]

>  <b>WILSON</b> `If I hadn't hauled back on the stick. I must have done it instinctively. Unless it was some atua, powerful joker in the sky.' <i>(1976,Pacific Star,92</i>)

>  <b>LADY MARTIN</b> He (Mr. Davis) told us that an Atua Maori, - that is to say, a man who professes to be possessed by a spirit, - had been disturbing the neighbourhood. <i>(1884,Our Maoris,35</i>)

>  When approached for rates, he stolidly asserted that New Zealand was first settled by the Maoris and all the land belonged 'to the Atua' (the Almighty), and they would pay no rates. <i>(Jul 19, 1924,NZ Truth,5</i>)

>  <b>REES</b> The old superstition of "Atua" and "Taipo" (exacting spirits of disease)pass away whenever Christian doctrines are received. <i>(1851,GB Parliamentary Papers (Irish Univ. series),IX 1779/33</i>)

>  <b>WAKEFIELD</b> ... the natives have a legend that [the Manawatu] was formed by an Atua, or `Evil Spirit', who was in the form of a large Totara-tree ... <i>(1848,Handbook for New Zealand, The,118</i>)

>  <b>MANING</b> ... what the relations wanted was that the atua, or familiar spirit of the tohunga, should bring the ship back into port ... <i>(1863,Old New Zealand,(ix) : 152</i>)

>  <b>TAYLOR</b> He therefore announced ... that there must first be a great karakia, or ceremony of worship, to induce the great Atua (spirit) to inspire them rightly as to what was to be done with the pakeha. <i>(Jan 27, 1865,Early Travellers,491</i>) [NOTES: Meade Ride through Disturbed Districts in 1959 Taylor Early Travellers]

>  <b>LADY MARTIN</b> He ... told us that an Atua Maori, - that is to say a man who professes to be possessed by a spirit, - had been disturbing the neighbourhood. <i>(1884,Our Maoris,35</i>) [NOTES: OEDS]

>  ... packed a Moriori Atua for Dr. Benjamin Moorhouse. It is a human figure after a fashion, scraped not cut on the bark of a kopi, that is the karaka of N.Z. <i>(May 30, 1904,Chudleigh Diary,422</i>) [NOTES: 1950 ed.]

>  If their karakia rites and remedies were found unavailing, the atua (demons) were considered implacable, and the diseased person was doomed to perish. <i>(1905,TrNZI,37/5</i>)

>  <b>COWAN</b> Puhi says he [a morepork] is an Atua, a god, and has a hundred eyes; ... <i>(1916,Bush Explorers,15</i>) [NOTES: TS:VUW]

>  <b>ANDERSEN</b> atua, god, demon, superatural being, ghost; anything malign, disagreeable. A Maori has said: Toku atua he Pakeha (My abomination is a Pakeha). <i>(1946,Journal of the Polynesian Society,55/151</i>) [NOTES: Maori Words in English]

>  atua : atua. [check this if used : it may not belong here] <i>(1947,Reeds' School Dictionary: NZ Suppl.,173</i>)

>  atua or akua n, pl atua or atuas or akua or akuas [Tahitian, Maori, or Samoan atua, Hawaiianakua] : a Polynesian supernatural being or spirit <i>(1961,Merriam-Webster Third International,142</i>)

>  <b>KEENE</b> atua: god, ghost. <i>(1963,O Te Raki,190</i>)

>  <b>SINCLAIR</b> atua god, demon <i>(1969,History of New Zealand,319</i>) [NOTES: [Maori glossary]

>  atua n. NZ supernatural being, god, demon (each tohunga diagnoses what his atua has revealed to him. [M] <i>(1986,NZ Pocket Oxford Dictionary,43</i>) [NOTES: nf. 1982 Collins Concise Eng. Dict.; 1985 Collins NZ Compact Dict.]

>  <b>MIKAERE</b> [Mitai Tuture records] Te Maiharoa went ahead to Awakino to kill the `taipo' of that place and I think he killed two there. Taipo is the name used now but we called [47] those evil spirits atua then [c1860s]. <i>(1988,Te Maiharoa,46</i>)

>  <b>SHORTLAND</b> The body of the first person slain was sacred to the `Atua' or spirit, who had guided them to success. <i>(1851,Southern Districts of NZ,68</i>)

>  It is known for certain that those seen are the atua , or spirit-like people, the Patupaearehe, &c., and not Tangata Maori or people of the Maori race <i>(1909,Journal of the Polynesian Society,III/34</i>)

>  The atua also acknowledge confrontation and turbulent times ... <i>(Oct 26, 2006,Wairarapa News,20</i>) [NOTES: Macron included.]

>  You are pretty sure that though he may have some faith in the atonement as a charm to exorcise `atuas' that [sic] his Xtianity does not go a great deal further. <i>(1851,Richmond-Atkinson Papers,1/103</i>) [NOTES: J.C. Richmond]

>  [The Brothers] was a tapu place where dwelt an atua or deity, who controlled the winds and seas. <i>(May 2, 1910,Dominion,6</i>)

>  <b>MACLEAN</b> Many people had tried to find Hapuakorari (the lake of the flaxsticks), but none succeeded because it was a sacred place, which only tohunga could visit and they had never revealed its location. Anyone else who neared it found themselves enveloped in thick cloud, summoned by the atua to hide the lake. <i>(1994,Tararua : the Story of a Mountain Range,68</i>)

>  <b>COLLINS</b> ... a gentle breeze of wind, which gives warning of its approach to an inferior Ea-tooa (or divinity) that hovers over the grave. .. In his chart Too-gee has marked an imaginary road which goes the lengthways of Ea-hei-no-mawe, viz. from Cook's Strait to the North Cape, which Too-gee calls Terry-inga. While the soul is received by the good Ea-tooa, an evil spirit is also in readiness to carry the impure part of the corpse to the above road, along which it is carried to Terry-inga, whence it is precipitated into the sea. <i>(1845,English Colony in NSW,345</i>)

>  <b>NICHOLAS</b> The Etua, (God) says he, rises upon him like a full moon, ... and passes by him like a shot from the cannon's mouth. <i>(1814,New Zealand (various authors/titles),I/65</i>)

>  <b>KENDALL in ELDER</b> Six days men were allowed to work, and every seventh day was appointed as a day of rest from labour, and to keakea atua; for this was the only term which we could then make use of in order to convey to their ideas our worshipping the Supreme Being. <i>Ibid.</i> 26 July 69 I think atua is their name for the Supreme Being, but I am not certain. <i>Ibid.</i> 11 Mar 75 The natives say if this should be allowed atua would be angry and a heavy calamity would befall them. <i>Ibid.</i> 77 Atua had then, as it was conceived, entered into him. Hence he was taboo (tapu) himself, or a sacred person. No New Zealander was permitted to come near him except the togungha (tohunga) or priest and those of his own family. <i>Ibid.</i> 14 July 138 The chiefs and elders of the people are called atua even whilst they are living. One aged friend, Terra (Tara) says that the god of thunder is in his forehead. <i>(Sep 6, 1814,Marsden's Lieutenants,59</i>)

>  <b>NICHOLAS</b> The Supreme Being Attu[acute]a nue, or Etua <i>(1817,New Zealand (various authors/titles),2/327</i>) [NOTES: English-NZ Vocabulary]

>  <b>CRUISE</b> Being cautious in concealing the object of meetings of this kind, the New Zealanders said it originated in the presence of an Atua, or god, and directed the gentlemen to the placewhere the divinity was to be found. <i>Ibid.</i> 11 May 97 Some of the shot had struck the rocks at no great distance from [the native]; for he told us that an Atua, or something wonderful, had been there. <i>Ibid.</i> 184 (Remarks) They believe in a Supreme being, designated the Atua, or something incomprehensible. <i>(May 10, 1820,Journal,95</i>) [NOTES: (1957) in 1823]

>  <b>KENDALL</b> [|prec.= acute, '= stress] At|ua, s. The Supreme Being. <i>(1820,Grammar & Vocabulary of the Language of NZ,137</i>)

>  <b>BOULTBEE</b> a spirit - a'ttooa [atua] <i>Ibid.</i> 285 (|= macron precedes) a spirit, a|ttooa [atua] <i>Ibid.</i> 189 ... an old [Maori] man returned thanks to the Attooa for the victory, ... <i>(1835,Journal,113</i>) [NOTES: c1826-1827, list of Maori words (1986)]

>  <b>Leigh in 1908 McNABB</b> After they had done this he assured them he would come and tabbatab (consecrate) the temple, and put into it the Atua (with some potato), after which the Atua (their god) would befed with the same, and then they might expect that the caterpillars would all go away, and the potatoes would grow again. <i>(Aug 4, 1830,Journal,I/709</i>) [NOTES: check spelling tabbytab or tabbatab]

>  <b>CRAIK</b> ... he was told by the tohunga, or priest, that if he presumed [153] to put one finger in his mouth ... the Atua (divinity) would certainly punish his impious contempt ... <i>(1830,New Zealanders, The,152</i>)

>  <b>WILLIAMS</b> The natives have expressed considerable alarm and have brought this forward as another evidence of the anger of the Atua for not observing more rigidly their religious ceremonies. <i>(May 16, 1832,Early Journals of Henry Williams, The,245</i>) [NOTES: 1961 ed.]

>  .. the first taken in battle they give to feed their Athna [sic], the name of their God ... <i>(1833,Sydney Monitor</i>) [NOTES: in 1913 McNab Old Whaling Days 54]

>  <b>MARKHAM</b> And some seem to think that the Missionaries pray to the Attuah [atua] `Spirit of God",/ to Kiki [kaikai] or `eat up" the people, ... [ed. fn] Strictly speaking this is not a Maori word but either a rendering of kakai, eat frequently, or merely an example of pidgin Maori. <i>Ibid.</i> 66 The old people believe that the (Atua) God of the Parkiars Strangers is killing or eating the Mouries or Natives and they see their strength decrease daily; ... [fn] The Old Men have an idea that the Atua of the Parkeiahs is kiki-ing or eating up their people or Nation ... <i>(1834,New Zealand (various authors/titles),53</i>) [NOTES: 1963 ed.]

>  <b>BOULTBEE</b> The adventures of the late Expedition again were related, and ... an old man returned thanks to the Attooa for the victory ... <i>(1835,Journal,70</i>) [NOTES: 1986 ed.]

>  <b>POLACK</b> They contented themselves by stating, that their supplications to the Taniwoa, or native Neptune, had alone been instrumental [20] in causing the disappearance of these new Atuas, who so materially differed from the native theogony; ... <i>(1838,New Zealand  (Polack),1/19</i>)

>  <b>J.C. BIDWILL</b> I said that a Pakiha could do no harm in going up, as no place was taboo to a Pakiha; that the taboo only applied to Mowries; and finally, that if the mountain was an atua, I must be a greater atua, or I could not have got to the top of it, ... <i>(1841,New Zealand  (Polack),85</i>) [NOTES: 1952 ed.]

>  <b>SELWYN</b> It appeared that various causes had led to the relapse of the chiefs, among which was the death of some of their children, which they attributed to the displeasure of their own Atua (spirit) at the introduction of Christianity. <i>(Dec 11, 1842,Journal,82</i>) [NOTES: Letter in 1847 NZ Part I (The Church in the Colonies). Also in 1842 Selwyn Journal in 1959 Taylor Early Travellers 11 Dec 79]

>  <b>DIEFFENBACH</b> The natives have a whimsical story of an `atua' (spirit), who they say was drowned here, and is still undergoing decomposition. <i>(1843,Travels in NZ,1/135</i>)

>  <b>ANGAS</b> Before the introduction of Christianity amongst them, they believed, [line missing somewhere] <i>(1847,Savage Life,1/331</i>)



## atwai
 
 <i>n.</i> (See [hutiwai](../H#hutiwai))



## aua
 
 <i>n.</i> yellow-eyed mullet, so-called <i>herring</i> (See [herring](../H#herring),[yellow-eyed mullet](../Y#yellow-eyed-mullet))

>  <b>WILLIAMS</b> The whole of the long range of the New Zealand Coast is abundantly supplied with beautiful fish. Of this variety of 78 species I will name a part, viz. ... Arwa [Awa] species of whiting; ... [<i>Glossary</i> 118 awa yellow-eyed mullet <i>Agonostomus fosteri</i>)] <i>(1843,NZ Journal,40</i>)

>  <b>WAKEFIELD</b> <i>Smelts</i> are abundant on some parts of the coast; both they and the fish called <i>herring, (aua </i>of the natives,) readily take a fly in the estuaries of rivers, which they enter with the tide. <i>Ibid.</i> 212 Fish of all kinds ... abound off the mouth of the [Wanganui] river; and the <i>kawai</i>, as well as smelts, and the <i>aua</i>, or so-called herring, ascend the river ... <i>(1848,Handbook for New Zealand, The,161</i>)

>  English Name Native Name ... ... Sprat or Sardine Aua <i>(Oct 5, 1849,NZ Journal,(Vol IX) 125</i>) [NOTES: quoting Anglo Maori Warder Oct 5]

>  <b>TAYLOR</b> <i>Awa</i>, is a small tidal river fish, resembling the roach; the settlers call it the herring, which it only resembles in shape. It is synonymous with <i> takeke</i>. [Wms 371 <i>takeke</i> 2. Smelt (Retropinna retropinna) (Whanganui)] <i>Ibid.</i> 624 The Fam. <i>Clupeadicae</i> is represented by the <i>Aua</i>, herring, which is plentiful in all the tidal rivers, and may be taken to any extent. <i>(1855,Te Ika a Maui,411</i>)

>  <b>HECTOR</b> 58. SEA MULLET. ... The Makawhiti [so Williams 169] or Aua of the Maoris (<i>Agonostoma Forsteri</i>) ... is a common fish ... It is commonly called Herring, from its general resemblance in size and form to that fish, but is easily recognisable from the true Herring by having two fins on the back, ... The Picton Herring, a dried fish commonly known throughout the colony, is the Aua preserved by smoking. <i>(1872,Edible Fishes,114</i>)

>  [Native or Common Name] Aua, Mullet, Herring . [Scientific Name] <i>Agonostoma forsteri</i> <i>(1877,TrNZI,9/485</i>) [NOTES: P. Thomson, Otago]

>  <b>SENIOR</b> Amongst the sea-fishes fit for food there are ... aua, rock cod, wrasse, and patiki; ... <i>(1880,Travel & Trout,288</i>)

>  The same species was subsequently identified as the true Picton herring of commerce, of which the first- received specimens were the aua or herring-mullet. <i>(1883,TrNZI,15/208</i>) [NOTES: Notes on Picton herring]

>  <b>BULLER</b> When the pouch is emptied, the mother [shag] ... goes off for a fresh supply of <i>auas</i>, whilst her offspring ... await her return. <i>(1888,Birds of New Zealand,2/151</i>)

>  Of the sea-fishes that are used as food in New Zealand, we have among the constant residents on all parts of our coast the hapuku, tarakihi, trevally, moki, aua, rock-cod, wrasse, and patiki; and while the snapper, mullet, and gurnet are only met with in the north, the trumpeter, butterfish, and red-cod are confined to the south. <i>(1892,NZ Official Handbook,167</i>)

>  <b>MORRIS</b> Aua, ...Maori name for a New Zealand fish, <i>Agonostoma forsteri</i>, Bleek. Another Maori name is <i>Makawhiti</i>; also called <i>Sea-mullet</i> and sometimes <i>Herring</i> (q.v.). It is abundant also in Tasmanian estuaries, and is one of the fishes which dried is called <i>Picton Herring</i> (q.v.). See also <i>Maray</i> and <i>Mullet. Agonostoma</i> is a genus of the family <i>Mugilidae</i> or Grey-Mullets. <i>(1898,Austral English,8</i>)

>  <i>... large numbers of aua or kataha</i> (Agonostoma forsteri)<i>; about 60 lb. ... weight of pilchard or mohimohi (Clupea sagax)</i>, two varieties; ... hundreds of young red-cod, rarii (<i>Lotella bacchus</i>), and what I believe are young rock-cod, or kokopu or rawaru (<i>Percis colias</i>). The red-cod were from 3 in. to 4 in. in length, and the rawaru, or, as the natives here call them, `toitoi' or `panepane', from 2 in. to 6 in. long. <i>(1903,TrNZI,35/319</i>) [NOTES: Mair Fish in Piako River]

>  <b>BEATTIE</b> Aua - mullet; ... Said to be the same as the kanae of the North Island. <i>(1945,Maori Place-Names of Canterbury,61</i>) [NOTES: South Island Maori Lists `Wahi Mahika Kai' in 1945 Beattie Maori Place-names of Canterbury]

>  <b>SHERRIN</b> AUA (see MAKAWHITI). <i>Ibid.</i> 65 MAKAWHITI (<i>Agonostoma Forsteri</i>). ... Called the sea-mullet in other places, but the makawhiti or aua by the Maoris. It is a common fish, Dr. Hector says, obtained at all seasons of the year by fishing from the wharves in the harbours. In Dunedin it is known as the mullet. ... The Picton herring, a dried fish, commonly known throughout the colony, is the aua, preserved by smoking. <i>(1886,Handbook Fishes of NZ,11</i>)

>  Kahawai ... live chiefly on the fish we call `sprats' here [at Helensville] (the green sea-mullet - aua, or kahawhiti, of the Maoris). <i>(1897,AJHR,H-17 : 5</i>) [NOTES: Hector Protection of Mullet]

>  A'.u-a (a.'.u-&adot.; colloq. ou'.&adot.), n. [Maori.] A New Zealand coast fish (<i>Agonostoma Forsteri</i>) of the Mullet family (Mugilidae). <i>(1898,Merriam-Webster Third International,2017</i>)

>  AGONOSTOMUS FORSTERI (Bloch and Schneider). <i>Common Mullet; Aua</i>. Common in the estuaries of all tidal rivers, often entering fresh water in the summer. Called `herring' at Golden Bay and on the west coast of the South Island, but not identical with Picton herring. ... Distribution: New Zealand harbours and estuaries. <i>(Jun 1921,NZ Journal of Science & Technology,IV/120</i>) [NOTES: Phillipps Notes on the Edible Fishes]

>  <b>PHILLIPPS</b> [AWA] <i>Agnostomus forsteri</i> (Cuv. & Val.). YELLOW-EYED MULLET, HERRING; AWA. <i>(1927,Bibliography of NZ Fishes,30</i>) [NOTES: 1971 ed.]

>  <b>POWELL</b> YELLOW-EYED MULLET (<i>Agnostomus forsteri</i>) Awa of the Maoris, is usually called the Herring, but it is not related to the English fish of that name. <i>(1947,Native Animals of NZ,67</i>)

>  <b>GRAHAM</b> YELLOW-EYED MULLET (AWA) <i>Aldrichetta forsteri</i> (Cuv. and Val.) The Yellow-eyed Mullet is caught and sold at times under the wrong name of Herring. It is not a Herring nor is it in any way related to the Herring family. The Herring has only one dorsal fin while the Yellow-eyed Mullet has two. <i>(1956,NZ Fishes: An Identification Guide,214</i>) [NOTES: 2nd ed.]

>  <b>PARROTT</b> THE YELLOW-EYED MULLET <i>Agonostomus forsteri</i> (Cuv. & Val.) ... Maori name: AWA. <i>(1957,Sea Anglers' Fishes of NZ,35</i>)

>  <b>DOOGUE & MORELAND</b> YELLOWEYE MULLET ... <i>Other names: Aldrichetta forsteri</i>; herring, common mullet, sprat; sea mullet, river mullet (Australia); aua (Maori). <i>(1966,Sea Anglers' Guide,220</i>)

>  <b>NATUSCH</b> [The grey mullet's], yellow-eyed relative, the awa (<i>Aldrichetta</i>, ...) is common. The group also includes the pike (<i>Sphyraena</i>, ...), ... <i>(1967,Native Animals of NZ,217</i>)

>  <b>THOMAS</b> The Maoris knew to a nicety when to expect the Aua, or herring, or to be correct, the yellow eyed mullet ... <i>(1970,Way Up North,24</i>)

>  Mullet are slender, bluish-grey and silver fish, ... New Zealand has two species : the grey mullet or <i>kanae</i>, and the yellow-eyed mullet or <i>awa</i> <i>(1982,Two Hundred Years of NZ Food & Cookery,78</i>)

## Auckland cove
 
 <i>n.</i> early nickname for an Aucklander

>  <b>THOMSON</b> ... it is worthy of notice, that the colonists, soon after the formation of all the settlements, acquired distinguishing epithets; thus there was an Auckland cove, a Wellington swell, A Nelson snob, a Taranaki exquisite, an Otago cockney, and a Canterbury pilgrim. These epithets, almost already forgotten, are too characteristic to be buried in oblivion, as each originated in some peculiarity of the leading colonists. The Otago settlers derived theirs from their ignorance of the country in which they lived; the Auckland people from their extensive Sydney connexions; andthe origin of the other names are sufficiently obvious without explanation. <i>(1859,Story of New Zealand, The,2/188</i>)



## Auckland eel
 
 <i>n.</i>

>  ANGUILLA AUCKLANDII. <i> Auckland Eel</i>. Found in the Auckland market during November and December. This eel has a distribution extending over the whole North Island. <i>(May 1922,NZ Journal of Science & Technology,V/94</i>) [NOTES: Phillipps and HodgkinsonFurther Notes on Edible Fishes]



## Auckland green gecko
 
 <i>n.</i> the common green gecko ocurring widely in central and northern North Island. [<i>Naultinus elegans elegans</i> aka common green gecko, aka elegant gecko] (See [common green gecko](../C#common-green-gecko))

>  The Auckland Green Gecko is found in the northern half of the North Island, north of Taranaki. The tail is very versatile, being used as a "fifth limb" to climb and for balance. It hunts by night for insects but also likes to sunbathe. The Auckland Green Gecko lives in scrub and forest areas, especially Kanuka and Manuka, also known as tea tree. <i>(2009,Internet - see Notes</i>) [NOTES: http://www.nhc.net.nz/index/lizards-new-zealand/auckland-green-gecko/auckland-green-gecko.htm No date given, 2009 is estimate]

>  The kit, produced by the Society for Research on Amphibians and Reptiles in New Zealand, is aimed at local authorities to ensure endemic reptiles, such as the iconic Auckland green gecko, are protected from urban development, or relocated in suitable surroundings. <i>(Feb 20, 2009,Internet - see Notes</i>) [NOTES: http://www.massey.ac.nz/massey/about-us/news/article.cfm?mnarticle=conference-highlights-threats-to-reptiles-and-amphibians-19-02-2009]

>  Two species of lizard will be released in a ceremony under Maori blessing to an "open sanctuary" being developed behind a 2.5km predator-proof fence over much of the 588ha coastal peninsula park. They include 12 threatened Auckland green geckos as well as more common grey and brown forest geckos, all gathered from the 7.5km route of the $365 million tolled motorway extension being built between Orewa and Puhoi. <i>(Nov 25, 2005,Internet - see Notes</i>) [NOTES: http://www.nzherald.co.nz/nz/news/article.cfm?c_id=1&objectid=10356911]

## Aucklands
 
 <i>n.</i> The Auckland Regiment

>  <b>WAITE</b> The Wellingtons were on the left, the Aucklands in the centre, and the Canterburys on the right. <i>(1919,New Zealanders at Gallipoli,125</i>)

>  <b>PUGSLEY</b> The Aucklands and Otagos grimly held on to the fringes of the woods. <i>Ibid.</i> 299 On the Apex.. the Otagos and remnants of the Auckland Infantry were subject to constant shell, rifle and sniper fire. <i>(1984,Gallipoli: the New Zealand Story,197</i>)



## auction block
 
 <i>n.</i> A system of hiring wharf labour from ??date to??ca1935. (See cit., bureau system.)

>  The Lyttelton waterfront has been working under the bureau system of engaging labour for nine years now. ... `And,' the bureau manager said, `the men wouldn't like to go back to the old way again.' Under the old system, foremen of the various shipping companies stood on a small platform or block with the men awaiting employment before them and nominated those they required to work their own ships. The trouble with this `auction block' system, as it was called, was that each company tended to give preference to a certain group of men. It engaged the same men first all the time and employed others only when the preferred group were all at work. This meant very often the available work on the waterfront was not evenly shared; and that's what the bureau system tries to avoid. <i>(1948,Our Own Country,91</i>) [NOTES: from Korero, AEWS magazine]

>  .. he obviously sought to engage strong, able, experienced men to get the work done quickly, but the "auction block" system of engaging labour lent itself to corruption and to charges of bribery, discimination and victimisation. <i>(1993,Wharfie,p12</i>)



## aue
 
 <i>exclam.</i> expressing astonishment or distress; alas! [ORIGIN: Maori]

>  <b>MANING</b> `Is it you? - is it you? - truly is it you? - aue! aue! they hold me, they restrain me; ...' <i>(1863,Old New Zealand,(x) 159</i>)

>  <b>GRIEVE</b> And here was the rangatira Pakeha a whole day before his time. Aue Aue. [fn] rangatira - a chief. Pakeha - a white person. Aue - a wailing expression of grief. <i>(1939,Sketches from Maoriland,46</i>) [NOTES: 1961 ed.]

>  aue or auwe interj [Maori, Hawaiian, Tahitian, Marquesan, & Samoan aue, auwe] - used in Polynesia to express an emotional reaction (as sorrow, surprise, or affection) <i>(1961,Merriam-Webster Third International,143</i>)

>  <b>KEENE</b> aue! alas! <i>(1963,O Te Raki,190</i>)

>  <b>MIDDLETON</b> `Aue!' growled Dad, smiting his thigh. <i>(1964,Walk on the Beach,171</i>) [NOTES: Not for a Seagull]

>  aue! ... exclamation of astonishment or distress. <i>(1968,NZ Contemp. Dict. Suppl. (Collins),2</i>)

>  <b>FINLAYSON</b> aue!: exclamation of excited astonishment or of dismay. <i>(1973,Brown Man's Burden,140</i>) [NOTES: Pearson's Glossary]

>  <b>HULME</b> [p] 10 Aue = exclamation of dismay, or despair <i>(1983,Bone People, The,465</i>) [NOTES: Gloss]

>  <b>IHIMAERA</b> Aue, if it wasn't for my bad leg I would have ridden astride my Blue Mist ... <i>(1986,Matriarch, The,65</i>)

>  <b>HULME</b> Aue, I know you recall. You lost your good temper and yelled at me all kinds of rude names and swearing, taureka and sloven, 'pokokohua and bitch. <i>(1986,Te Kaihau,115</i>)

>  <b>BURCHFIELD</b> aue /|awe:/, interj. N.Z. [Maori.] An exclamation of astonishment, distress, etc.; alas! Cf. Hawaiian `auwe, which is illustrated by quots. from 1938 to 1972 in F.G. Cassidy Dict. Amer. Regional English (1985) q.v. 1934 in WEBSTER (labelled Polynesian). <i>(1988,Lexicographical & Linguistic Studies,192</i>)

>  <b>FINLAYSON</b> The shock of its fall has shaken the earth and loosened the roots of the lesser trees. `Aue!aue! aue!' `Aue! aue! aue!' echoed the moruners in their anguished keen. <i>(1938,Brown Man's Burden,48</i>)

>  <b>IHIMAERA</b> Aue, Never mind; Pita Mahana didn't have all his kids for nothing. <i>(1974,Whanau,V.22</i>)

>  <b>B. MASON</b> Aue, the secrets remain. <i>(Aug 1978,Islands,15</i>)

>  <b>HULME</b> ah hell, the fucking word has its place, but all the time? ... aue. <i>(1984,Bone People, The,I. 12</i>) [NOTES: 1985 ed.]

>  `No Aunty,' said Tom. `I don't know Wellington. I thought you fellas would no all that!' `Aue!' ... `Hey, does anyone know how to get to Government House?' `You mean the Beehive, Aunty?' <i>(1988,Short Stories from NZ,133</i>)

>  <b>CAMPBELL</b> Aue! I shall be the crown upon your brow, my warriors the cloak about your shoulders ... <i>(1955,By Reef and Range,14</i>)

## aukati
 
 <i>n.</i> . [Aukati ... 3. n. Line which one may not pass.] Mainly as attrib., with boundary, line, pole, a line which may not be passed or crossed. (Cf cut n. f. Ma. kati.)

>  David Young.. referred to the mythical aukati (boundary) between Maori and Pakeha history, and accused Belich of crossing it. <i>(Sep 23, 1989,Listener (New Zealand Listener),7</i>)

>  The solution was to draw an aukati line around the floor, dividing tapu from noa. <i>(Nov 23, 2005,NZ Herald,B5</i>)

>  I had dinner with them [sc. M.P.s], for the "aukati" was as distinctly marked in this two-pounds-a-night hovel as in the Parliamentary Chambers. <i>(Jun 11, 1887,Auckland Weekly News,21</i>)

>  <b>J.C.Richmond</b> On their side the chiefs to withdraw their ukati and openly and public[ly] disavow ... the murderous tribes of Hauhau, to allow needful roads and royal rights over the territory etc. <i>(Feb 26, 1869,Richmond-Atkinson Papers,2/281</i>)

>  <b>COWAN</b> ... not a pakeha farm ... southward of the Puniu, the old Aukati pole. <i>(1916,Bush Explorers,1</i>) [NOTES: TS:VUW]

>  <b>COWAN</b> The Kingites strictly enforced their aukati - that is, they forbade pakeha intrusion on the Maori side of the frontier line. <i>(1922,NZ Wars, The,2/468</i>) [NOTES: 1983 ed.]

>  <b>COWAN</b> Sometimes the farthest-out farmers went across the Aukati line to trade with Maoris ... and they were on friendly terms with many of the Kingites. <i>(1935,Hero Stories of NZ,158</i>)

>  <b>COWAN</b> But they enforced the `Aukati' order of the Kingite chiefs against the pakeha. <i>(1940,Sir Donald Maclean,113</i>)

>  <b>NORRIS</b> The Waikato adherents of the Maori King ... kept all Europeans from crossing the Puniu River ... The firmness of this decision was emphasised by the deaths of Europeans who had ... crossed the Aukati Line, the boundary over which the white people were forbidden to pass. <i>(1964,Settlers in Depression,49</i>)

>  <b>BELICH</b> aukati boundary, line which one does not cross <i>(1986,NZ Wars, The,337</i>) [NOTES: Maori Glossary]

>  But the Maori Wars and the aukati closed the area until the latter was lifted and the railway was again pushed southwards. <i>(Jul 1955,NZ Geog. Society Rec.,July-Sep 6</i>)

## Aussie
 
 <i>adj.</i> (informal) Australian

>  Sydney-born artist Tom Mutch had the Aussiest of reasons for fetching up in New Zealand as a twenty-year-old in the early 1970's. Notes <i>(2006,NZ House & Garden,Vol / page 58</i>)

>  <b>BAKER</b> We use bonzer and Aussie almost as naturally as the Australians. <i>(1941,NZ Slang,62</i>)

>  The Kiwi way is to say `She's jake' and take what comes. The Kiwi doesn't hit back. The Aussie hits back. Why else do the English, with their dreadful accents, think the Aussie accent is sodreadful? <i>(1966,NZ Short Stories,103</i>) [NOTES: Ballantyne `Other Gardens']



 
 <i>n.</i> (informal) Australia.

## Aussie bag
 
 <i>n.</i> a plastic bag to assist people to commit suicide

>  ...Australian euthanasia campaigner Philip Nitschke planned to distribute the "Aussie bags" when he visited New Zealand to hold euthanasia workshops, possibly this year. The controversial bags, made by the Exit Australia group which Dr Nitschke founded, have an elasticised opening and are based on a Canadian design. They are aimed at terminally ill or severely incapacitated people who want to die because thet are in pain or in distress. <i>(Aug 8, 2002,Dominion Post,A8</i>)

## Austral
 
 <i>adj.</i> Australasian, esp. in phr. Austral English

>  austral english n, cap A & E : the language of most inhabitants of Australia and New Zealand - used esp. with the implication that it is a variety of English distinct from that used in Great Britain yet not so divergent as to be a separate language; compare AMERICAN ENGLISH, AUSTRALIAN ENGLISH, BRITISH ENGLISH <i>(1961,Memories of Maoriland,145</i>)

>  AUSTRAL TRIFLE ... sandwich cake ... whipped cream ... passionfruit ... jelly ... walnuts ... put one round of the sandwich cake in a glass dish ... whipped cream on it, ... other half of cake on top ... spread the passionfruit ... when jelly is nearly set pour it over the whole ... whipped cream on top ... <i>(1928,Davis Dainty Dishes,20</i>)
